MARK FOY,
Of Sydney, is now having One Thousand young
Parrots taught to say the following words—
“ You will get it cheaper at Mark Foy’s.”
And these birds will be liberated throughout the Australian
country districts.
Don’t shoot a “ Mark Foy Parrot.”
Their advice is golden to all.

“THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated
(which is published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
The Permanent Force.
These are books deserving, in Bacon’s
phrase, “ to be read and digested ” —books
which every thoughtful or cultured man
should own and study.
Socialism, by Graham. Cloth, 5s 6d. The
best book on the subject.
The Study of Sociology, by'Herbert Spencer.
Cloth, 6s 6d. The mind-essence of a
mental giant.
The History of the Conflict between Religion
and Science, by Prof. Draper. Cloth, 5s 6d.
Man and Woman, by Havelock Ellis. Cloth,
6s 6d. An up-to-date comparison, re-
markably interesting.
The Evolution of Sex, by Geddes and Thom-
son. Cloth, 4s. A masterly book.
The Criminal, by Havelock Ellis. Cloth, 4s.
The best popular monograph.
The Man of Genius, by Lombroso (cloth, 4s).
Brilliant and fascinating.
Fabian Essays in Socialism. Paper, Is 3d.
A Plea for Liberty. Paper, 2s 3d.
Conventional Lies of Our Civilisation, by
Max Nordau. Paper, 3s 6d, Strong
meat for men.
A Yankee at the Court of King Arthur, by
Mark Twain. Boards, 2s 6d.
The Story of an African Farm, by Olive
Schreiner. Cloth, 4s. Unique in power
and fascination. A very nice edition.
Tristram Shandy, by Laurence Sterne; two
vols.; cloth, 4s each. A beautiful edition.
Thackeray’s Vanity Fair ; well-printed, plain
cloth binding, 2s 6d.
Poems, by Walt Whitman. Cloth, Is 3d.
The poet of Democracy.
Matthew Arnold’s Poems ; complete edition,
8s (companion to above).
Cynicus, His Humor and Satire. Paper,
Is 3d. Clever and suggestive cartoons.
Tennyson’s Poems ; complete edition, well
printed and cloth-bound, 8s.
Wordsworth’s Poems ; complete edition, 8s
(companion to above).
Shakspere’s Works. The Mignon edition ;
6 vols. in box, 22s. Admirable for pre-
sentation ; and a charming edition to own.
American__Authors.
“The David Douglas series of American
Authors ” is charmingly printed, and the
books are small and compact, suitable for the
pocket. Posted at Is 3d each volume, paper
covers ; or cloth extra, 2s 3d.

BY A BUSH BOOKSELLER.
What do bush readers buy ? Well, here’s a bush
reader coming into the shop now. Look at him !
Red hair, all over his face; dirty clothes; and a
Atrone smell of old pipes about him. Now, listen !
“ D’ye sell books, misther ?”
“Yes.”
“ ’Av’ yer got Willv Ro-o-i-illy ?”
“No.”
“ ’Av’ yer got ‘ The Red Hand of Ulsther ?•”
“No.”
“Well, ’av’ yer got ‘ Spaches from the Dock’ ? ”
“No.”
“ Shure, yerz got no books at all.”
There, you see, he leaves after making a spittoon
of the floor ; and smell the tobacco-juice ! Here’s
Another.
“ Have you got ‘ Maria Monk ’ ?”
“No, I don’t keep such trash.”
“You’re a mug, then ; you’d sell whips of
them. Anyhow, what d’ye want for ‘Ned
Kelly?’ ”
, “Two shillings.”
“ Two shillings ! All right, I’ll take it.”
He’s off —and my matches, too, from the
counter. Watch this fellow with the gaiters !
“ I want a decent book.”
“There you are, have a look round.”
“ Right, go on with your biz ; I’ll just run over
them. ”
Hear his profane remarks ! He’s “ read them
of course.
“ What fools buy these ? ”
(Wait
“ Hav.- y. m jrot ‘ Robbery Under Arms ? ’ ”
“ I sold the last yesterday.”
“Why, strike me dead! there’s one in the
window.”
“ Oh, I beg your pardon, I forgot that one.”
Now I’ll stack the books up again. . . Look
At this woman with the large family !
“ I want something to read—you know, mister,
something interesting and lively.” (Makes one
wonder how she can find time to read with all
those youngsters.) “ I’ll leave it to you to recom-
mend, sir.”
“Well, here’s some of Miss Braddon’s.”
“Oh, I’ve read them.”
“ Well, here’s a work, ma’am, that every woman
should read. It was suppressed some time ago;
it’s by Mrs. Besant.”
“ What, the lady that was lecturing in Sydney
the other day ? ”
“ Yes.”
“ How much?”
Sold ! It’s wonderful how quick a “suppressed”
work will sell. Then another class of people won’t
lead a book unless it’s brought under their notice
in some peculiar manner. Here’s an instance.
We have a racehorse here called Jack Sheppard,
who won the big race last meeting. I happened
to have a lot of Ainsworth’s “ Jack Sheppard ” on
hand which I couldn’t sell before, but after the
races I sold them in a fortnight. The buyers
must have thought it a sporting yarn.
A mild-spoken man came in one day and said,
“ I see you’ve got a grand assortment of books.”
“ Middling,” said I.
He bent over the counter and whispered, “ Have
you got anything blue ?”
“ Blue ! No? I haven’t got any Blue-Books;
they’re all yellow-backs.”
With a look of contempt he left the shop. I
was green then, and wondered what he meant;
but I’ve had plenty customers of the same sort
since.
The “Young Lad i es’ Journal, ” * ‘Family Herald, ”
And “Bow Bells” kind of literature is most in
demand with female readers—which, no doubt.
Accounts for the aristocratic names of the little
girls who attend our State schools. It’s sur-
prising what a number of Cissys, Mauds, Ethels,
Gwendolines, Ediths, and Gladys there are, com-
e,red with the Marys, Marthas, Janes, and
ates. (No one ever christens a daughter Brid-
get nowadays).
Dream books? Yes, and books of fate, mar-
riage guides, fortune-telling books, and so on.
Tbe girls simply doat on them.
No use recommending any of the standard
authors—Dickens, Thackeray, Lytton. Don’t
seem to suit most bush-readers. In fact, I’ve
found it’s not good policy to recommend a book at
all. I just say : “No, I don’t like it myself; I’ve
read it, but too much blood about it for me.” Off
it goes at once !
Woman authors are bad stock, no matter how
flaring the picture on the cover. Poetry is a dead
letter, though a few years ago I sold a “ Byron ”
to a giddy youth. I started a lending-library
onee, but soon got full of it. I mean I got empty
of it—books as well.
The only boom in my book trade was when
Deeming was being tried. Everybody wanted to
know when his life was to be published, with full
details. Of course I booked a lot of orders. Since
then it’s all detective tales, bushranging yarns,
and heroes of the prize-ring. I asked one of my
customers if he’d read “Sherlock Holmes.”
“ Who the is he ?” he growled. I counter-
manded the order to the wholesale house at once,
as I didn’t care about waiting so long for the book
to get popular amongst my far-off patrons. I
ordered two dozen “ Newgate Calendars ” instead.
That’s always popular—the standard work of tbe
bush.
We have a few enquiries for “ Mysteries of the
Court of London.” I shall order some. I try to
supply all my customers’ literary wants, and I
think I have fairly succeeded ; but when they
want “ Maria Monk,” “The Escaped Nun,” and
“ blue books,” I’m not on. No ; I refer them to
the School of Arts.

“The mutiny of the Bounty was not
caused by Bligh, but waa because of attachments
formed by the sailors for women of Otaheite.”—
Encyclopedia Bkitannica.
Dear Bulletin, —An interesting subject
for you ; How used N.S.W. aborigines bury their
dead ? The blacks of the South Coast, at
Moruya, Bateman’s Bay, and Nelligen, used to
carry their dead about with them for several
weeks, stretched full-length in a sheet of bark,
which, as the corpse shrivelled up, they tightened
and lashed with stringy-bark or vines. They
never buried their dead near the place where
he or she died; and when they did dispose
of the corpse they sought a hollow tree, in
whose barrel they dropped it—immediately
clearing out to watch the spot for several
weeks from the highest location they could reach.
Many years before I was born, the Nelligen
(N. S. W.) blacks “ buried ” one of their dead in
this manner at one of the late Mr. Nolan’s cattle-
stations. My late father was in charge, and as
the stench from the corpse was unbearable to him
and his hut-keeper, they cut a hole one evening in
the butt of the tree and set fire to it. The blacks
saw the fire, and, as this was their method of
signalling to each other, they rushed down in war-
paint to the number of about 400, but, not in time
to save their friend, who was effectually cremated.
Only about 16 years ago the skeleton of an
aborigine was found in a tree at Bodalla, and I
have heard of others being found east of Braid-
woo 1.
H. J. M'COOKY.

Our Ideas are Bigi
We get bigger every day; on Saturday night
we are bigger than on the previous Monday,
and on the Wednesday following we are bigger
still. We can’t help growing. We have all the
elements of growth. The soil we are planted in
is good, the climate propitious, the people kind.
We are continually watered by the lifegiving
showers of a nation’s approval. Can anyone
wonder that we grow?
Our growth is a wonderful thing. People
describe it as magical. There is no magic in it.
Anyone, or any set of people, can do the same if
they do as we have done. But they don’t; they
can’t find the way, charge too much for the privi-
lege of doing business with them, and generally
and deplorably make a mess of the whole affair.
That’s the mischief.
Most People Like Us! All Believe in Us!
Not a Few Swear by Us!
It’s cupboard love. We suit them, make their
money go a long way, and generally render the
path of life an easy one. There’s no sentiment
about it. It’s real biz, and no mistake. In that
way we are beloved throughout Australia, the
measure erf affection each one bestows upon us
being gauged by the amount of benefit received.
The more you buy at our store the greater your
liking for us will become, because the more you
invest with us the better dividends you receive.
If living IN THE COUNTRY, send for our
Catalogue and Patterns of anything you require ;
if in town, call every day.

A GOLDEN SHANTY.’
“The Bulletin" Scrap Book.
THE GARRET.
O, it was here that love his gifts bestowed
Dn youth’s wild age !
Gladly once more I seek my youth’s abode
In pilgrimage:
Here my young mistress with her poet dared
Reckless to dwell.
She was sixteen, I twenty, and we shared
This attio cell.
Yes, twas a garret! be it known to all
Here was love’s shrine.
1 here read in charcoal, traced along the wall,
1 he unfinished line ;
Here was the board where kindred hearts would
blend ;
The Jew can tell
How oft I pawned my watch to feast a friend
In attic cell.
O’ my Lisette’s fair form could I recall
With fairy wand !
There she would blind the window with her shawl,
Bashful, yet fond.
What tho from whom she got her dress I’ve since
Learned but too well,
Still in those days I envied not a prince
In attic cell.
Here the glad tidings on our banquet burst
Mid the bright bowls :
Yes it vas here Marengo’s triumph first
Kindled our souls!
Bronze cannon roared, France with redoubled
might
Felt her heart swell!
Proudly we drank our Consul’s health that night
In attic cell.
Dream of my youthful days, I’d freely give.
Ere my life’s close,
All the dull days I’m destined yet to live
For one of those.
Where shall I now find raptures that were felt
Joys that befell
And hopes that dawned at twenty when I dwelt—
In attic cell.
Biranger—Englished by Father Mahoney.
A correspondent writes to Otago Wit-
ness (M.L.) on the vastness and resource of the
.Scotch language as compared with the scantiness
of the English :—
You can say in English “ a large dog,” or “ a small
dog,” but there you’re done. In Scotch you can sav—
A doag.
A wee doag.
A wee bit doag.
A wee bit bitty doag.
A we bit doagity doag.
A sma’ doag.
A inuckle doag.
A language with so many different shades of dog
in it deserves to be treated with respect.
Dear Bulletin,— From J. M. Barrie’s
memorial poem on the late Robert L. Stevenson :
We made the doakens walk the plank
That free I like to gar you loup.
One of these days a competent ITinglish satirist,
of the W. S. Gilbert type, will expose the humbug
of modern Scotcli lingo verse. He may even have
the temerity to suggest that a good deal of Robbie
Burns is drivel in disguise. Anyway, it is clear
that a Scawtch versifier has an unfair pull over his
brother born South of the Tweed, because he can
rhyme “round” with “ground” one minute and
“roun”’ with “town” the next. Take Bobbie
B.’s “The Cotter’s Saturday Night ” :
Belyve the elder bairns come drapping in,
At sen ice out, amang the farrmrs roun’ ;
Some ca’ the pleugh, some herd, some tentie rin
A cannie errand to a neebor town.
A little further on it suits the immortal Robert to
talk the language of Shakespeare :
O happy love ! where love like this is found!
O heartfelt raptures ! bliss beyond compare 1
I’ve paced much this weary mortal round,
And sage Experience bids me this declare.
Take the same overrated Scotchman’s last verse of
“ A Vision ” :
He sang vvi’ joy the former day,
He weeping wail’d his latter times
But what he said it was nae play
I winna venture’t in my rhj mes.
Isn’t that a drivelling conclusion '! What would
one say to a bard who wrote :
His song erstwhile in gladness woke
The day ; he wail’d his latter times;
But what he said it was no joke,
I dare not put it in my rhymes.
Yours, Cockney.

Medical etiquette is a dodge for keeping
the business in the hands of the older men. It’s
to hold the young men back and to stop the holes
by which they might slip through to the front. —
Dr. Conan Doyie.
All the rage in Paris. Latest Parisian

The project to establish a new daily morn-
ing paper in Brisbane is so far afoot that premises
and machinery have been secured, and a start
will be made as soon as the time is judged pro-
pitious. Architect Gailey, who isn’t over well
pleased with the treatment he got from the
Courier people, is a leading supporter of the ven-
ture, which is said to be backed by several sub-
rosa politicians who can’t trust the existing press
—all butterers of Boodlewraith. The new journal
is to be “independent ” (of course); and its career
will depend chiefly upon the amount of brains at
the back of it.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with -postage added where required—
-which you may pay by Post O[lice Order, One Pound
Sole, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for

This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, hut is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with ivhom ive do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. P.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, MARCH 9, 1895.
"The Bulletin’s” Motion of Censure.
LaSt week the N.S. Wales Opposition,turned
up with the usual motion to the effect that
the country was “dissatisfied” with the
present Government. The motion was dis-
tinctly illogical, for to ask Parliament to
decide whether the country was or was not
dissatisfied with the Government implied
that the matter was open to doubt, and that
the country might be really satisfied after
all. As the country always is dissatisfied
with the Government, this aspect of the case
is supremely ridiculous. The question to be
settled in every want-of-confidence debate is
whether the country objects more strongly
to the existing Ministry than it thinks
it would to any other Ministry which it
is likely to get —in other words, whether it
is more aggravated at the Cabinet than
usual; and, as no one can really tell how
aggravated the country usually is, even this
is a matter which is difficult of decision. The
fact that the nation always objects to the
Government is sufficiently demonstrated by
the fact that almost every Government holds
office until the very first opportunity which
the country can find of ejecting it, and no
longer. And the fact that the country
always objects to the Opposition is similarly
evidenced by the fact that, when it becomes
a Government, it is also ejected just as soon
)is the nation can find a chance to get rid of
it. Therefore, the life of every Cabinet is
divided into three stages : first, the stage in
which the nation objects to the Opposition
rather more than it does to the Ministry ;
second, that in which the country objects to
them both equally ; third, that in which it
objects to the Ministry rather more than it
does to the Opposition.
« * •
The troubles and incoherencies of Party
Government are many, and when a sad,
ejected politician rises to state that the
country isn’t satisfied with the Ministry —as
if any country was ever satisfied with any
Ministry—they are intensified rather than
alleviated. The Ministry, as a rule, doesn’t
know what the country wants. Neither
does the Opposition. The country, in many
cases, doesn’t know with any degree of cer-
tainty what it wants itself, and when it does
know it has no means of informing its Parlia-
mentary representatives. All it can do is
to .elect them, and leave them to find out
to which of their opinions their election
was owing. They never do find out,
however, for the necessary machinery
for making the discovery doesn’t exist.
Parliament, in its turn, has no definite
means of letting the Government know what
it wants, save by ejecting it. When the
Ministry learns by an adverse vote the real
opinion of the House on some important
question, it has straightway to leave office
and go where it can’t make any use of its
newly-acquired information. Often, how-
ever, a Ministry is ejected on a general vote
of “ dissatisfaction,” and then it leaves office
without knowing why it is turned out, and
the other party comes in without knowing
why it has come in. The House simply
states that it thinks that the country thinks
that the Cabinet has done something it
shouldn’t have done, and the new Govern-
ment comes in on the ground that the House
believes the country is of opinion it should
do something which its predecessor didn’t
do, or not do something which its prede-
cessor did. A general motion of dissatis-
faction conveys less valuable information
than anything else in the domain of Party
Government.
* • »
In these years of financial leanness all
Ministries come into office with a general
instruction to retrench and make the finances
square, or at least to make them look square.
This is the one thing that is definitely under-
stood where everything else is vague and
shadowy. Also, most Ministries go out of
office on the ground—among others —that
they didn’t retrench ; the rest go out on the
ground—among others —that they did re-
trench. A retrenching Ministry, like every
other, holds its precarious situation from
hour to hour, by the votes of its individual
supporters, and these supporters have to be
individually placated by not retrenching their
friends or relatives or constituents, and by
finding billets for their friends and relatives
and constituents, and by <Constructing public
works in their electorates, and other expen-
sive devices. The average Cabinet keeps in
office by doing these things, and when the
inevitable deficiency arrives it is ejected for
doing them by the same people who would
otherwise have ejected it for not doing them.
A Ministry is a particularly wretched species
of public servant which is always liable to be
dismissed at a moment’s notice for not carry-
iug out instructions, which, as a rule, it
didn’t receive, and it fails to get these in-
structions from a body of men who are also
acting on instructions which they didn’t
receive. Also, its degradation is emphasised
by the constant accusation that it hangs on
to office for the sake of the salary attached.
Which accusation, by the way, is almost
invariably true ; everybody—even raonarchs
and bishops and scavengers—hangs on
to office for the sake of the attached salary,
and even the scavenger’s billet would be a
precarious one if the other scavenger, who
wanted it, could get it by moving that the
present holder of the job didn’t possess the
confidence of the country.
The average Ministry is weak, because it
doesn’t know what the country wants it to
do, or what the Pailiament wants it to do,
and because it isn’t appointed by anyone in
particular, but comes into existence by a
process of secret negotiation, which is known
as “forming the Cabinet.'’ Parliament has
a right to express its disapproval of the
Cabinet after it is formed by the simple pro-
cess of knocking it on the head on the ground
that it doesn’t possess “the confidence of
the House,” but it has no open or effective
method of indicating what kind of Cabinet
will possess the confidence of the House.
One of the most common reasons why the
Government loses the confidence of the
House is because it doesn’t make satis-
factory progress with public business,
but all the forms of Parliament appear to
be specially devised to make any serious
progress absolutely impossible. The avowed
reason for the existence of the Opposition is
to block legislation in every possible way,
and its facilities for so doing are unlimited.
In particular, it may come forward any num-
ber of times in this session with a motion
that the House is dissatisfied because the
Government doesn’t get along faster, and
may, with reasonably clever generalship,
occupy a week or a fortnight with a desultory
conversation about the delay of public busi-
ness. When the motion is disposed of, and
business shows some signs of getting started,
there is nothing to prevent it immediately
starting another long discussion about the
unprogressiveness of public business. One
of the chief reasons why no Government ever
makes reasonable progress with anything is
the time wasted in calling attention to the
fact that it doesn’t make progress. The rest
of the time is largely occupied with motions
for adjournment, and with doing all over
again business which was done in the previous
session, and then lost through being left
unfinished when the prorogation came along
and released the exhausted senators from
their labours.
• * *
A Ministry which was appointed by Par-
liament for a fixed period—which could re-
ceive definite instructions from Parliament
without being ejected and which was
directed by a Parliament which had itself
received definite instructions from the people
through the Referendum, or some similar
device which would allow the nation to give
a definite opinion, might be reasonably
expected to do something. The present
kind of Ministry can only do anything when
circumstances are unusually favorable, and
even then it does it painfully and slowly,
and to move that the country is not satisfied
with it is mere supererogation, for no country
in its senses can ■ be satisfied with any
Ministry of the present pattern. An elec-
tive Government could afford to retrench
where needed, for its acts could only be
reversed by a specific action of the House.
The member who had been turned into an
enemy because a railway hadn’t been built in
his electorate, and the one whose nephew
had been retrenched out of the public ser-
vice, and the one who hadn’t contrived to
sell a piece of land to the State 'at an
exorbitant price, could no longer pool
their grievances and get rid of the
Government under a general vote of
dissatisfaction, for the Government would be
immoveable until the expiration of its term
of office. Their only mode of redress would
be by asking the House to instruct the
Government by a specific vote to build the
railway, and buy the piece of land at an ex-
cessive value, and to find billets for the
relatives in question. And no Parliament,
with the fear of a general election before its
eyes, dare do such a thing ; also, no poli-
tician, with the prospect of a hereafter when
he next met his constituents, dare ask it to
do so. Also, there would be no more want
of confidence motions, for a Government
with a fixed billet could get along without
Parliamentary confidence. The reasons why
Australia should adopt the Referendum
and the system of elective Cabinets are
many, but the most effective reason of all
is that it would put Parliament, for the first
time, on a business like basis. It would enable
the nation to give plain instructions to its
representatives, and it would enable Parlia-
ment to carry out these instructions with
reasonable promptness, instead of being per-
petually switched off on irrelevant side-issues
and political free-fights of no public interest.
Meanwhile, at time of writing, the New
South Wales Parliament is still endeavouring
to decide whether the country is or is not
dissatisfied with the present Ministry, which
fact might as well be taken for granted and
much time and eloquence saved thereby.
The country is dissatisfied with the present
Ministry and with every other Ministry, and
with the present Parliament and every other
Parliament, and with itself, and its political
system, and with the universe and things in
general.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Squatter and Shearer—Again !
The Pastoralists’ Union “ sentence,” like
Belial’s, “is for open war.” Let Australia
mark it. Last season’s shearing was completed
at great loss to employers and employes, owing
to the strikes and lock-outs caused by the pas-
toralists’ arbitrary repudiation—without refer-
ence to the other party in the agreement—of
the platform which both parties at the ’9l Con-
ference bound themselves to observe. Outrages
and alleged outrages, assaults and reprisals,
were attributed to each side freely. In the
encounter the peace of the community was
often broken, and its prosperity interrupted.
Large additional expense was incurred by
Governments, and paid by the people. In-
tent to avoid similar hostilities next season,
if it be possible, the Workers’ Union
Conference, which sat last week at Albury,
approached the New South Wales Pas-
toralists’ Union, endeavouring to reach a
modus vivendi. The Pastoralists’ reply was to
the effect that no change of plan was" contem-
plated, no discussion of matters in dispute
could take place. The P.U. thus with a light
heart takes the responsibility of a fresh struggle
this year. It disdains compromise ; it rejects
the opportunity of reconciliation. Good. The
P.U. has the right to do all these things—the
technical right, the legal right. A man may
conduct his own business as he pleases, says the
P.U. He may, and he shall—as long as his
pleasure does not interfere with the pleasure of
the society of which he is a member. If it does,
then that man must go under. The members
of the P.U. as individuals are entitled to the
utmost license, while they proceed on lines
approved as beneficial to the whole community.
The moment they go outside those lines, their
liberty is a dead letter. This is both sound
morality and common sense. “This is a free
country,” says the P.U. It is ; and it is also
not a free country. No man is free to enslave
another, to rob another, to kill another. On a
petty scale, conduct calculated to provoke a breach
of the peace is an offence recognised at law.
The conduct of the P.U. is emphatically calcu-
lated to provoke a breach of the peace on a
grand scale. Considering all the circumstances
the organisation of the opposing parties ; the
history of the dispute; the contingencies
involved— The Bulletin believes that, in
obstinately standing on their rights as indivi-
duals, and in callously refusing to recognise
responsibilities to the community which pro-
tects those rights, the associated pastoralists—
or the coterie by which they are bossed
—are acting as enemies of the public
safety. Let them be treated accordingly.
The pastoralists claim their rights : give
them their rights. Let them have their
pound of flesh—and call them to heavy account
for every drop of blood shed. If this year also
industry is paralysed, “outrages” are reported,
the country is thrown into turmoil, then make
the pastoralists foot the bill. Man by man—
wretched ledger items that they often are !
the squatters would probably avoid the strife
if they could. It is with the force of the banka
and finance companies—the tyranny of capital,
the brutality of concentrated Eat—that Aus-
tralia has to reckon, and most of those banks
are the reconstructed legacies of ’93, kept open
only by the backing of the people’s credit, now
using the people’s credit to crush the people !
If Parliament once makes it clear that, so long
as the present attitude is persisted in, the State
is ranged on the side of Labor, and will
pitilessly exact its dues from, and utterly deny
its favors to, the opponents of Labor, the situa-
tion will change speedily and permanently for
the belter.

The Fro-trade Freetectionist.
The following letter, published in the Age, by
Alex. Caughey, a Victorian wine-grower, who
declared himself before the Tariff' Commission
a thorough-going Freetrader, but demanded
Protection for his own industry, is interesting,
as showing the average Freetrader’s attitude :
I was pleased to read in one of your leading articles in
to-day s paper your remarks on the evidence given by me
before the Tariff Commission, as it gives me the oppor-
tunity of explaining why I, as a Freetrader, can consist-
ently urge on that commission the maintenance of the
present rate of duty on foreign wines, and I will even go
further and say that if they are studying the in-
terest of the colony at large they should at the
same time abandon the duty charged on all the
appliances used in the trade, such as bottles, corks,
capsules, wine presses, wine pumps, &c. Every Govern-
ment of late years, and all the press of the colony are
unanimous in the opinion that what we want is to make
our soil so productive as to yield not only plenty of labor,
but such a surplus for remunerative export as will bring
wealth to the country—and there is no industry as yet
started amongst us which is more likely to carry out this
result. The lately-published statistics show the result
that there are at present under vines in the colony
30,275 acres, 20,118 in full bearing, and 10,157 coming for-
ward. In round numbers, an average of one man to each
10 acres is required for the tillage and gathering <>f the crop;
therefore, even in the small progress made, 3000 men are
employed in addition to the large amount of labor outside
the vineyard. The distribution of wages must, therefore,
amount to upwards of £200,000. I find that the duty col-
lected on foreign wines in the year 1893 was £10,528105. Id.
at the present duly, and in 1891, at the 6s. duty, it was
£25,846 19s. 3d., making a difference in favor of the lower
duty of £15,218 9s. 2d., and as Mr, Jackson in his evidence
pointed out this lower duty was gradually decreasing, the
question therefore arises whether it is of more importance
to the country to extend and encourage the growth of
vines and establish a permanent source of wealth and treat
foreign wines as a luxury, which even Freetraders admit
should be taxed ; or to secure fqr a few years the extra
duty which would result from the reduction, and
which itself is problematical. The vinegrowers have
no hope that the 12s. duty will increase the price of
their produce ; but, as I stated in evidence, as late presi-
dent of the Australian Wine Association and present
chairman of the Board of Viticulture, from my intimacy
for the last 25 years with the wine-growers and trade
generally, I am in a position to say that the increase in
duty has led to an increased consumption of Australian
wim s, and a general hope is expressed that a few years
will overcome all prejudice amongst local consumers.
Mr. Caughey, as a Freetrader, wants his own
industry protected because Protection—applied
to his own industry—increases production,
supplies work and wages to a vast number of
inhabitants, “ establishes a permanent source
of wealth,” and doesn’t increase the price to
the consumer. The Bulletin, as a Protec-
tionist paper, wants Caughey’s industry pro-
tected for exactly the same reasons. Only
Caughey, as a Freetrader, w r ants everybody
else’s industry to be allowed to go to perdition
in its own way on these sound Freetrade prin-
ciples, which apply to every business save his
own ; while The Bulletin, as a Protectionist,
wants to foster Caughey’s industry and every-
body else’s as well. And that is just where the
difference between Freetrade and Protection
comes in.
The Tory Wag Once More.
The audacious humor of the wag who writes
leaders for Tory dailies shows no evidence of
decline. Hoax is piled upon hoax. How far
the humor can be carried is not certain, but it
has lately prompted the Argus to the doctrine
that the minimum wage must be followed by
the State guarantee of an adequate salary to
every man in any trade or profession he chooses.
Then would the State have to find work for 40 musicians
and one cook; fora hundred poets and three laborers.
Thousands of young fellows would be watchmakers,
engravers, architects, and so on. None would be coal-
heavers, or seamen, or farm hands.
This certainly is an exquisite conception—a
community producing nothing to eat, drink or
wear, promulgating a law to supply itself out
of vacancy with the necessaries of life. The
minimum wage leads to this as surely as the
agitation against “sweating” has ultimate
regard to the wasting of jockeys. In the king-
dom of the blind the one-eyed man can wink his
other eye, and among Tories no hoax is too
childish to be believed. The minimum wage is
a temporary expedient whereby the worker is
secured a certain percentage of the fruits of his
labor. It is a temporary expedient in an age
of expediency, and will probably be scorned in
future days as a half-measure. At present
labor fetches the market-price when it should
fetch the market-value. The minimum wage is
a compromise between the two. But the agita-
tion for the compromise with injustice in the
interest of justice will not be altogether barren
if it continue to suggest further mad hoaxes on
the Tories.
The Lawyer and the Bootmaker.
William Morris, bootmaker, is a sorrowful
illustration of what may happen to a small
Democrat who opposes a large Liberal at a
Victorian Parliamentary election. The Liberal
in this case was Alfred Deakin, a friend of
the Age, and the Age variously described the
intruder as “a pledged Tory decoy duck,” a
“ Tory fox,” a “ tool of the Conservatives ” and
“a traitor,” although he was merely an ill-
advised working man who didn’t believe in
Bearin', and thought himself a more desirable
candidate than the lawyer in question. Morris
sued the mistaken newspaper for defamation of
public character. He came through his case in
a creditable manner. It was clearly shown that
he had worked 4 4 on his own,” wi th a little financial
assistance from a hotel-keeper who encouraged
struggling candidates because committee meet-
ings and election excitement are good business
for his pub. Morris was neither the decoy
duck of Tory M 4 Cracken, nor a traitor to
Deakin’s Liberal cause. However, the Age
accused the harmless man of so much per-
fidy that he called upon Mr. David Syme


“ The .Fiscal Superstition,” by Max Hirsch. Published
by E. W. Cole, of Melbourne, Sydney, and Adelaide.
itho day before the election offering to
retire in Deakin’s favor, and leave the question
of expenses to Deakin’s committee. There-
upon the Age proprietor telegraphed to his
friend Deakin, who wired back a refusal to
] ave any dealings with Morris. By thib time,
of course, Deakin reckoned that the small
democratic candidate had been smashed by Age
thunderbolts. David Syme thought Deakin
was unwise and would have only himself to
blame if he lost the election, yet next morning’s
Age repeated the story that Morris was run-
ning in the Tory interest. Which, coming
after the man’s hona-fide offer to merge himself
into Deakin, was surely not fair comment. A
jury of four gave Morris a verdict for one
farthing damages, instead of awarding him a
modest sum sufficient to carry costs. He has
to pay his own costs or owe them. The jury
estimated the value of Morris’s political repu-
tation by the small number of votes he obtained.
They evidently forgot to consider the damage
done to his prospects by the Age newspaper,
and they saw something ridiculous in his oppo-
sition to a fashionable Liberal of Deakin’s
calibre. It was the bootmaker’s misfortune to
be measured with Deakin and set down as a
candidate of no account. Counsel had worked
upon the jury’s weakness in this respect by
praising Deakin to the skies. Morris was the
presumptuous creature who dreamed of cutting-
out a godlike lawyer. The magnitude of his
failure dwarfed his grievance against the
journal that libelled him. The libel was con-
siderable, but the bootmaker seemed small, so
he got a farthing.
The imprisoned bank-directors are getting out
of gaol one by one. The two latest to be
released are Low and Hadfield, of the
Northumberland Banking Co., one of the ugliest
and most shameless concerns of its kind, and an
affair which about reached the climax in the
matter of cooked balance-sheets, and vanished
assets, and general disaster. The two bankers
were sentenced to seven years in gaol, and are
discharged after serving rather more than two.
The reason of their release isn’t announced, but
possibly the fact that one of them is related to
an eminently respectable bishop has something
to do with the matter. At all events, the
man not related to a bishop who is
sentenced to seven years for stealing one
broken-down horse, almost invariably puts in
his full term, and when the small criminal is
kept in so vigorously and the the large one gets
out so regularly, it suggests a very natural
inference. For the 40th time—no prisoner’s
sentence should be commuted without the fact
being announced in the Government Gazette,
together with the reasons for the commutation.
Also, the commutation of sentences
solely with a Court of Appeal, instead of
being left in the hands of politicians whose
billets depend on their pleasing Parliamentary
supporters, and constituents, and so on,
and who can be privately interviewed, and
wept over by relatives, and approached in
all manner of ways. The result of the present
system is simply that the man who has no rela-
tives, or no influential relatives, remains in
gaol for his full term, and the other man who
has important connections, or who has somebody
to weep gracefully for him in the right quarter,
doesn’t. The release of Low and Hadfield
(who is related to a bishop) may be perfectly
just; The Bulletin, not knowing the recent
history of the case, doesn’t pretend to say
that it isn’t. But, if it is, the continued incar-
ceration of many other men is an outrage.
A deputation of parsons, headed by Bishop
Thornton, waited upon Ballarat City Council
the other day to protest against the running of
Sunday excursion trains to Queenscliff, where-
upon uprose the irreverent councillor Elliott
and chided Bishop “Samuel, Ballarat,” for
breaking the Sabbath by employing his groom
and coachman on Sunday, and keeping his cook
and servants at work throughout that blessed
day. The Bishop gasped and squirmed, and
the chokered deputation goggled its eyes and
said, “ Well, it never !” and the meek and
lowly members of the Council appeared on the
verge of having fits, but the Mucked Elliott
stuck to his guns and asked again with some
heat what the gaitered and shovel-hatted gentle-
man meant by it, anyhow. At this stage the
Mayor broke in, pale and distressed, and humbly
apologised to the Bishop for “the indignity ” that
had been heaped upon him ; whereupon Dr.
Thornton blew his nose with a loud report,
looked resigned, as became a man of God, and
said, “ he at least would take no further notice
of what had taken place.” Which goes to show
that an eel isn’t in it with an unctuous parson
when it comes to M’riggling out of a tight place,
the man, bishop or otherwise, who is driven
round by a liveried minion on the day of rest,
and whose portly cook has to stew for hours
before great fires pulley - hauling the saint’s
victuals on that same day, has no manner of
right to snort at the secular individual who
rides in a train on the blessed Sabbath. It is
merely a question of the manner of breaking
the commandments, and The Bulletin contends
that in a free country every man should have
the privilege of breaking his commandments
according to his own taste and inclination, pro-
viding he is within the law. The parsons
dictate our virtue to us ; shall they dictate
our sins ?
Vic. Spectator (Wesleyan Church organ) :
A vigorous application of the lash as a punishment for
hrutal offences against the person has a most salutary
effect. Offenders of this class are almost always cowardly
mutes, who are most effectually reached through their
S , "u vvas refreshing to read in the daily papers, last
week, how the flogging of one of them in Sydney moved
nm to howls, and tears, and fervent prayers. Our own
J unkins would be all the better for a similar experience.
Hus is the Church’s rendering of “Ye have
heard that it was said, an eye for an eye, and a
tooth for a tooth; but I say unto you, resist
Rot him that is evil: but whosoever smiteth
thee on thy right cheek, turn to him the other
also.” Perhaps herein lies the solution of the
problem, “ Why don’t men go to church V”
Make absence a floggable offence. If the
lash will move one sinner to “ fervent
prayer ” it ought to be equally effective
. found. It is notable that your Methody
pietist does not advocate the lash for the snealj-
thief offences peculiar to his own order. When
we hear some sleek saint barracking for the cat
as above, we always think of the humorist who
sent the other fellow a libel-writ for having
called him a professional Hardshell—“ meaning
thereby a smug person who uses religion as a
cloak for robbery, and who for base purposes
inveigles trustful girls to Sunday-school pic-
nics.” Why shouldn’t the lash be applied to
the religious confidence-tricksters ? To be
tied up and partially skinned alive is even
terrible to the pugilist specially trained
to bear punishment ; but how the fear of
a flaying would oppress the alleged souls of
the broadclothed and belltoppered vessels of
grace, whose tying balance-sheets and fraudful
bank-dodgery have choked the asylums with
madmen and the rivers with suicides, and in-
creased by a thousand per cent, the hopeless,
ragged wretches who mumble to themselves as
they wander in the streets ! No; the lash is for
the man in moleskins and for the man in mole-
skins only.
Dr. Moore, R.O. Bishop of Ballarat:
We find the greatest talents and most expensive attain-
ments assooiafed in the same individual with the most
deplorable crimes. What sad examples of this class do
we see in Victoria, where religion is divorced from secular
knowledge.
Surety this is not a dig at poor, dear Matthias
Larkin, the man who embezzled South Mel-
bourne, and who was a profitable and devout
son of mother Church ! It is noteworthy, too,
that most of the boom brigands were good and
holy men, with a copious command of Scripture
and a fine pulpit style, and every superlative
rascal who makes his appearance in the dock
has a score of “respectable” witnesses —
usually including a parson or two—to attest
to his pious upbringing, and point out that he
was a regular attendant at church, and that,
consequently, his crime must have been a visita-
tion of the devil. The Victorian State school
is willing to compare criminals with the Sunday-
school, and chance it.
♦ *
“ The absolute and imperative duty of the
politician was not to make laws but to find put
what laws existed and to get his individual life
and the Government of the country in which he
lived in line with those laws.” Frank Cotton’s
late “Law and Order” lecture dropped this
and other perils to the world’s common sense.
Let Judge Forbes speak, as recently at Albury :
“ That is the law, but a monstrously bad law,
and I’ll not follow it. I dismiss the appeal
with costs and you can go to the higher court if
you like, but you’ll not get me to follow such a
law.” Judge Forbes is not an extraordinarily
wise moralist, but he is quite good enough to
hit Frank Cotton with. By the way, was not
Cotton, when he delivered that lecture, under
expectation of being nominated to New South
Wales Upper House ?
The Reid Government’s manipulation of the
dissolution-bogey proves what almost all New
South Wales Governments have also proved,
that the negotiations between a Governor and
his Premier should, as regards this right to a
dissolution, be related officially to the House
before the session begins. A Premier either
has or has not the right to claim a dissolution
as part of the compact between himself and the
Governor. If he has obtained the right it
should, as a portion of the government of the
people by the people, and not by star-chamber or
backstairs courtesans, be formally made known
to Parliament as soon as members assemble.
Many good objects would be attained thereby.
If, on the other hand, a Premier has not ob-
tained the right there would be no room for
the indecent bluff with which important votes
in the House are accompanied. As it is, a
Premier’s myrmidon sets afloat a rumor to the
effect that the Governor lias granted a right to
dissolve, and Parliament has no means of ascer-
taining whether the rumor is fouudationless or
not; the Premier, as befits a situation so
stupidly undemocratic, winks or breathes hard
when asked, or by some other oracular ex-
pedient shaves the wind of mendacity. An
official declaration of the compact with the
Governor is then democratically necessary, and
especially necessary where poker-politicians are
so much in evidence.
The New South Wales Parliamentary oppo-
nents of O’Sullivan’s attempt to legally pro-
tect newspapers in the publication of accurate
reports of public meetings w r ere singularly
obtuse in the application they made of their
“ man of straw ” argument. It is conceivable
that a vicious paper might load up a “ Garden
Honey” or an “ Old Dad” with defamatory
matter and afterwards report the utterances
under shelter of O’Sullivan’s bill; but if it did
so its offence would be, not the publishing of a
libel, but conspiring to defame, or instigating a
breach of the law—a much more serious offence
than ordinary libel. A man last week, in a
Sydney court, received 10 years’ hard labor for
inciting to murder ; he was not auctioned out
or imprisoned as a debtor, but was tried and
sentenced as a criminal. Inciting to defame is
in the same category, and is no more met by
seizing the type and printing-press of the
newspaper than would inciting to murder have
been met by a fine.
The circumstances attendant on the conviction
of the lad Bonner for complicity in the burn-
ing of the steamer Rodney during the recent
New South Wales Western shearing-troubles
are simply shocking. Bonner was tried at
Wilcannia, but, the jury disagreeing, he was
brought to Sydney for a second trial. A con-
stable swore that during his incarceration at
Wilcannia —after the first trial—he obtained a
confession from accused which lie, the con-
stable, afterwards reduced to writing, but
which alleged confession prisoner did not sign,
nor was it even read over to him. Accused,
when forced into the box at the second trial,
swore that he never made the alleged confes-
sion, but had said that he wished “ the affair
settled one way or the other.” On cross-
examination he admitted that he had served a
sentence of six months for housebreaking. It
would be hard indeed to find words capable of
expressing the bad logic and cruelty of a
system under which a man, legally stockwhipped
into the box through fear of an adverse infer-
ence being drawn from his silence, is cross-
examined before conviction as to a legally-
expiated offence. Despite accused’s denial the
alleged confession had weight with the jury,
who found a verd'ct of guilty, which verdict
was accentuated by Mr. Justice Simpson, who,
notwithstanding a recommendation to mercy,
sent Bonner into penal servitude for seven
years. Bonner may be guilty, but the method
of his conviction is degrading to the popular
conception of justice.
The Reid Government (N.S. W.) complains
bitterly about being obstructed by Dibbs’s latest
motion of censure. The Reid party fired about
a dozen motions of censure at Dibbs during his
two troubled years of office, but this fact doesn’t
make it feel any better. The motion of censure,
as at present conducted, is a supremely idiotic
business anyhow. As a rule, it involves a stop-
page of all work for anywhere from three to
fourteen days, a long, desultory conversation
about everything that the present Ministry and
all previous Ministries have done or said, or left
undone or unsaid, or which they should have
said or left unsaid, or done or omitted to do,
also about people and things in general, and
about all the social and political theories under
the sun. Then both sides seek to buy up
waverers by promises of all sorts, and the
party which loses asserts, with perfect truth,
that the other party won by corrupt means,
and the party which wins says, also with
perfect truth, that the other party tried to
win by corrupt means. Also, if the Govern-
ment comes out on top, its excuse there-
after for everything it fails to do is the loss
of time caused by the other party’s merce-
nary grabbing after office. So long as Party
Government endures, motions of censure seem
unavoidable, but at all events they should be
fixed on a new basis. When notice of such a
motion is given, Parliamentary business should
go on as usual. When the motion comes on,
all debate should be prohibited. Every member
who hasn’t got leave of absence from the House,
or who isn’t represented by a doctor’s certificate
of illness, or who isn’t away being tried before a
court of law, should be required to turn up under
penalty of ,£2O fine, and at the hour fixed a
division should take place. Then after five
minutes has been allowed, so that the winning
party may hooray over its victory, business
should go straight ahead, and no more motions
of censure be allowed for at least a month.
Some stirring events are expected in Q. politics
this year. The heads of the Labor-party are
said to have at last perceived that they can
achieve nothing as long as they keep in ex-
tremes, and are casting about for a modus
vivendi with the Opposition. The defection of
the ready and plausible Kingsbury from the
Ministerial side is promised by the quid-nuncs ;
and combined L.P., Opposition, and renegades
are to present a broad Democratic programme
to the Country, which is confidently expected
to overset Nelson. Likely enough, but the
L.P. must take care it isn’t made a catspaw for
Kingsbury’s chestnuts. That lengthy schemer
is emphatically a politician to distrust, and he
would do almost anything to get into office—for
which his career and character prove him demo-
cratically unfit.
Max Hirsch’s Superstition.
“The Fiscal Superstition,” by Max Hirsch, the
travelling boomer of the Vic. importing interest,
is a small but remarkable book, devoted to prop-
ping up Max’s favorite superstition—Free-
trade. In a general way it is the Voice of Max
calling out garbled statistics in the wilderness, no
one heeding him. A circular which accompanies
the volume says that Mr. Hirsch is “ recognised as
one of the leading political economists of the
colonies,” a statement which fairly expresses the
opinion the writer appears to hold of himself ; and
in the preface, Mr. Hirsch states that his facts
“ have been carefully collected and examined and
are reliable,” and are mainly based upon
Hayter’s and Coghlan’s statistics. This is the
most interesting statement of the lot, for a
more outrageously and foolishly-garbled set of
alleged facts and figures has never come under
The Bulletin’s notice. The two things most
visible in Mr. Hirsch’s volume are its in-
accuracy and its unutterable gall. The writer’s
object is to institute a comparison between New
South Wales and Victoria. He starts apparently
on the assumption that the past good fortune of
the latter province was owing solely to borrowed
money and the boom, and its present ill-fortune is
wholly owing to Protection—the breaking of the
boom having nothing whatever to do with it.
Likewise the good fortune of New South Wales
—such as it was—is wholly owing to Freetrade ;
and borrowed money and Protection—despite the
fact that N.S.W. has borrowed even more than
Victoiia, and is now a protected province—have
no connection with the matter whatever. Then
Mr. Hirsch plunges into a mass of evidence which
is interesting, though not in the way the writer
evidently intended.
# * #
It is “absolutely ludicrous,” says Mr. Hirsch
(p. 17) to maintain that manufacturers compete with
importers. Evidently the Vie. manufacturer must
compete with somebody, for everybody competes
with somebody, but whom he competes with the
writer doesn’t profess to say. He merely dumps
down his own impossible assertion and passes on.
He quotes a number of witnesses before the Vic.
Tariff Commission who said that the prices of
goods in their lines iiad been increased by Protec-
tion, and carefully suppresses all the witnesses,
and these were not few, who attested the con-
trary. On the cement question (p, 29) he declares
that the price of cement is much higher than it
was before the duty was imposed, and on the next
page he shows that the present price, with a
heavy duty, is 11s. 6d. per cask, and that in the
old Freetrade days it ranged as high as 16s. On
page 32 he points out that the Vic. price of reaper
and binder twine, which pays duty, hasfallen 47per
cent, in five years, while (page 36) seaming twine,
which pays no duty, has fallen only 33 per cent,
in 28 years, and then he calmly calls attention to
this” as an example of the special cheapness of
Freetrade. Apparently, this is done on the off-
chance that his readers won’t check the calcula-
tion. Also, his figures inadvertently show that the
dutiable reaper and binder twine is now much
cheaper than it was before the duty was imposed,
and he states that “during the season 1893-94 the
price of best Manilla twine was 6d. in Tasmania
(where there is no duty) as against Bd. in Victoria.”
Seeing that on the same page the 1894 price in
Victoria is quoted at 5Jd. instead of Bd., this
requires considerable assurance.
• * *
There are in Victoria, says Hirsfch (page 14),
only one ammunition and cartridge factory (six
according to Hay ter of 1893, pp. 276-279),
one paper mill (Hayter says there are two),
one dynamite factory (six according to Hay-
ter), one salt works (Hayter finds four), one
varnish factory (Hayter reports two), and one
candle works (Hayter alleges that there are 33).
I rotection, Hirsch alleges (page 39), has raised
wheat freights to London from 21s. Bd. in 1890 to
255. 7jd. in 1894. As V ictoria was protected for
20 years before 1890 it is quite as fair to assume
that it was Protection which reduced freights to
the unprecedented 21s. Bd. in the first place, but
Max suppresses this view of the case. He also fails
to mention that freights have also risen in the
other provinces—in fact, he flatly denies this. Pro-
tection, he says (page 39), has increased Vie. rail-
way freights also, which statement requires a con-
siderable amount of nerve, in the face of the re-
ductions made at various times for a good many
years past. “Itis a well-know n fact,” says Max,
“that two engines and two trucks now cost the
Department as much as three engines and three
trucks would cost under Freetrade conditions.”
Considering that all Government imports are free
of duty, and that Hirsch asserts that Protection
has reduced wages, the Vic. Government does work
under 1 reetrade conditions, but Max, seemingly,
isn t aware of his own discrepancy. Also, he
gives no figures or evidence whatever—merely bald
assertion. He further asserts (page 40): “that
railway freights are lower in N.S.W. than
Victoria is a notorious fact.” According to
Hayter (page 157), Victorian freights are lower
than those of N.S.W., and Hirsch’s statement has
been so often disproved that it badly wants
some reliable figures to support it. Protec-
tion, says the same gifted writer (page 41),
has killed the Victorian meat-preserving and
fellmongering industries. Hayter (page 270)
reports the fellmongering business as being
in full blast, but this doesn’t matter much.
On page 47, Hirsch announces that Freetrade
N.S.W., which had 9000 less factory operatives
than Victoria in 1888 and 10,000 less in 1890, had,
in 1893, actually 1100 more, and he points to this
as a proof that it is Freetrade, not Protection,
which builds up manufactures. The astonishing
Max actually professes not to know that N.S.W.
was a protected country when she thus got ahead
of her old-time enemy. Also, he points out (page
49) that Victoria includes as a factory any place,
regardless of the number of employes, where opera-
tions are carried on by steam or mechanical
power, and thereby swells her returns, but he
entirely suppresses the fact (Hayter, page 281) that
on this swelled basis Victoria employed 88,000
persons in factories, against N.S.W.’s 75,000.
That is to say, he seemingly reckons in his table
only the number of persons employed in factories
as the term is defined by law, while professing to
reckon all persons employed in factories, using the
word in the largest and widest sense. Further,
Hirsch’s Victorian figures for 1892 are seemingly
Hayter’s figures for 1893, and for 1888 he seems to
have tacked nearly 6000 non-existent operatives
on to Hayter’s figures, apparently through ignor-
ance, or else from a desire to make the subsequent
fall seem all the more violent. Also, his 1891 figures
appear to be Hayter’s figures for ’92, and he com-
pares the 1891 figures for N.S.W. with the 1892
figures for Victoria, and produces strange and
dreadful results thereby.
* * *
On page 45 the talented Hirsch gives a state-
ment of the exports and imports of Victorian and
N.S.W. native produce for a series of years,
showing that the Freetrade province is enor-
mously ahead of its Protectionist rival. The
export figures for N.S.W. have been taken with
tolerable accuracy from an ancient Customs return
a more recent one by Coghlan, which reduces some
of them considerably, has been carefully avoided.
Ihe Victorian figures, however, when compand
with Hayter’s figures (Hayter for ’93, page 69).
look as if Mr. Hirsch had deducted .£1,500,0(0
to £3,000,000 each year—at all events, about
-£25,000,000 has been knocked off the Vic. ex pot t;
in ten years, and a beautiful case for Freetrade
has thus been constructed. Whether the imports
are equally inaccurate The Bulletin doesn’t
know, not having the official figures available at
the moment. The Vic. soil, says the “Fiscal
Superstition ” man (page 51), is “richer and more
fertile than that of N.S. W. Coghlan’s agii-
cultural statistics (“Seven Colonies” for 1894,
pp. 191-217) show the exact contrary, but
Max Hirsch probably knows a lot more than
Coghlan.
• • •
( On page 50 Hirsch announces that, in 1893, New
South Wales “employed 44,757 more laborers”
than Victoria. Coghlan (page 248 of the “Seven
Colonies”) reports that Victoria, with a smaller
population, finds work for 7000 more males and
18,000 more females, but Hirsch, apparently,
makes his own statistics as already mentioned.
On page 56 he says that the protective period in
Victoria was from ’73 to ’93, and on page 58, when
it is convenient to fix things differently to support
a new batch of statements, he puts down
the beginning of “ effective ” Protection at
the year 1880. He understates (page 58) the
cultivated area of New South Wales in 1880
by 4000 acres and overstates it in 1890 by 250,000
acres (Coghlan’s Statistical Register, 1892), and
thereby swells the alleged increase in N.S.W.
under Freetrade by 254,000 acres. This is
managed by including fallow land in 1890,
and carefully leaving it out in 1880. Then
he goes on to point out that Freetrade booms
agriculture in a truly remarkable fashion. In a
comparative list of wages he takes the N.S.W.
rates from the Government statistics, but instead
of taking the Victorian rates from the same
reliable authority—which would utterly blast his
argument that Protection pays lower wages than
Freetrade—he gets the latter set of figures from
“careful personal enquiry.” Any kind of figures
can be got by careful personal enquiry, provided
the enquirer is sufficiently careful to enquire of the
person who will give him the figures he wants. He
omits all figures showing the enormous (protected)
agricultural production of Victoria. He persists, on
every occasion, in ignoring the fact that N.S.W.
is a protected country, and insists on regarding
it as purely Freetrade. He produces a cloud of
garbled numerals to show that Vic. manufactures
are steadily declining, while those of N.S.W. are
increasing and are far ahead of her neighbour’s,
and he peacefully drops the fact that the value
of Vic. manufactures is =£22,000,000, for the
latest year where returns are available, against
a total of .£16,000,000 for N.S.W. Wherever The
Bulletin has taken the trouble to check Mr.
Hirsch’s figures it has found him hopelessly wrong
—whether purposely wrong, or wrong through
mere ignorance, bigotry, and arithmetical in-
capacity, it doesn’t pretend to know. But, as a
“leading political economist,” Max should now
take a back seat for the rest of his life.

Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
A careful study of “ The Fatal Card,” at
Sydney Royal, leaves various impressions on the
miml, the first of these being that Haddon
Chambers and partner wrote their drama on a
sticky wicket, so to speak, and that the bowling
was shamefully uneven. Taking one considera-
tion with another, the good points of the piece
outnumber the bad ones, but still there are several
bad ones, and they stick out conspicuously. The
first and most glaring anachronism is the pivot on
which the whole play turns. In the first act, the
honorable Colorado villain, when he parts with
the hero—who has just saved his life—tears up a
card and gives him half of it so that they may re-
cognise each other when they meet again. They
are perfectly well acquainted, but the whole story
would be spoiled if they agreed to recognise each
other by sight in the ordinary way. So when the
same>orowd is thrown together again in England,
though the other people know each other readily
enough, the hero and the honorable villain—this
is to distinguish him from the crawlsome villain—
who have hardly changed in the least, meet over
and over again without a gleam of recognition.
• * #
Out of six people who led a wild, sinful
life in Colorado, two recognise a third one without
difficulty, and three know two of the crowd at
first sight, and one spots another as soon as he
sees her, but the people with the torn card about
them are blankly oblivions. Then, when Cosgrove
goes stealthily into an office to chloroform an old
man, he carries his luggage in a bag instead of
having it ready, and has to unfasten the bag and
get out the handkerchief, and uncork the chloro-
form, and pour it over the rag while he stands
behind his victim. Only the most patient kind of
victim would wait to be chloroformed in this
fashion. Also, the most interesting character in
lie drama walks out of it in act 4, and isn’t again
heard of.
• • »
Still, there is a good deal of novelty, much
fine scenery, and a lot of thrill in the “Fatal
Card.” It includes a lynching scene in Colorado,
a grateful and gentlemanly villain and a muddy
kind of ruffian named Jim, who acts under his
orders, a brutal and picturesque murder, an
explosion which blows a cottage to atoms
and buries Albert Norman among the ruins,
and a scene in which a demented poetess
takes away Bland Holt’s clothes and leaves
him naked behind a tree, in which situation he
declares his love to a lady through a knot-hole
and is accepted. The dialogue is very clever in
places, especially in those places where the leading
villain holds forth to Jim, and frankly states his
opinion of that pasty-looking character, and the
plot is ingenious, barring the discrepancies already
mentioned.
* * •
The new drama is staged in a fashion which
is even more lavish than usual, and the oast doesn’t
contain one character of any importance who calls
for just execration. Bland Holt and Baker are
both excellent. Albert Norman as the picturesque
villain, and Cosgrove as the pasty ruffian of
hog-like instincts, are commendable, and Scot
Inglis—who has little to do save wear an Irish
accent and worship the ground on which Miss
Hilda Spong treads with her adorable little feet—
does both satisfactorily. Also, he makes a bril-
liant retreat just before the explosion, and retreats
right out of the story. Miss Hilda Spong makes
Albert Norman’s saintly daughter a thine
of light and loveliness generally ; and
the bad woman, by Miss Vivienne, is
a powerful and picturesque character. To save
space, apparently, the bad woman goes into the
next room to take off her hat in Act 4 and never
returns, and her absence in the explosion scene—
where she ought to weep over Norman’s dead
body—is a thing to be regretted. Mrs. Bland
Holt, who doesn’t properly belong to the drama,
fills the part of a pretty little woman with much
success, and the demented poetess, and a few
subsidiary persons, can pass muster. The point
on which the “ Fatal Card ” gets ahead of the
ordinary melodrama is the dialogue, which is dis-
tinctly above the average. Also, the curtain
always falls on an effective climax. The ordinary
melodramatist’s idea of an effective climax is
generally a dead body, and in this respect Cham-
bers and Co. are a pleasing exception to the usual
rule.
*» # *
The revival of the “Second Mrs. Tan-
queray” at Sydney Lyceum only differed from
the original production of a year ago ip two par-
ticulars. Cecil Ward took the part of Capt.
Ardale, in place of the exited Renouf ; and Dor-
rington filled the character of George Orreyed—
who mjght be called Horrid for fhort—formerly
occupied by the gifted Cecil. The first change
was decidedly for the better, for Mr. Ward does
excellent work in his one brief appearance. The
second is, at all events, not a change for the worse.
The drill ky baronet has little to do save stack
himself in a ridiculous attitude on the sofa, and
Dorrington’s great wilderness of limbs lends itself
naturally to the attitude. Also, he has to stumble
out of the room in a bad state of intoxication, and
Dorrington-s fuddled rush for the door is a brief
and hurried work of art. Mrs. Brough’s Paula
lanqueray has, possibly, gained a little in in-
tensity since her first appearance in the character;
at all events her rendering of the fated
courtesan, who dies of her first and last
attempt to go straight and of the misery which
results thereby, was a tragedy which could hardly
be improved upon in the smallest particular.
Especially it reached high-water mark in the
last scene of all, where Paula looks into thefuture
and sees herself old and wrinkled and painted and
false-haired and decked with shop-teeth, having
abandoned the attempt to live down her old reputa-
tion, and with nothing to do save moulder and
think of the festive, sinful past. When she and
litheradge sat together on their two chairs and
wailed over this unspeakable future, even the d.c.
-—yes, even the Sydney Lyceum d.c. !!!! —stopped
all conversation and listened in dead and awful
silence. Titheradge was as good as ever, and the
same strikingly original remark applies likewise
to Dot Boucicault and Misses Temple and Noble,
• * •
On Friday (March 8) Mrs. Brough takes a
benefit, and the ever-popular “Niobe” will be
staged for the occasion. Then the beneficiaire
leaves for England on a holiday of uncertain
duration, and under these circumstances Friday
nights performance at the Lyceum should be a
stupendous function, and if there is one vacant
seat, or one vacant patch of standing-room, it will
be a reflection upon the character of Australia.
Mrs. Brough has played continuously in this
country for 10 years ; she has tilled an immense
number of characters in comedy, tragedy, drama,
opera, and other things as well, and has filled all
of them well, and most of them still better; and
she has probably given the community a larger
amount of refined pleasure and unalloyed satisfac-
tion than any other lady on the Australian stage,
t herefore The Bulletin bespeaks for her an en-
thusiastic farewell, and asks the d.c. to make as
little noise as possible with its feet and large
mouth when it comes in late as usual.

rest at the end of this week. It will re-open on

** * *
M'elb. Bijou was somewhat crowded on
Saturday, when “Esther Sand raz ” came up for
consideration. So the star of the Dacres would
seem to befin the ascendant. “ Either Sandraz ”
is Sydney Grundy’s warning to men who marry
lawful youqg wives for money, after having lost
their hearts to unlawful loves. Here the badly-
treated mistress is a Spanish lady, with a deep
regard for her protector until she gets left. Then
she registers a vengeful oath. By means of old
letters, Esther forces herself into the new home of
their author, and insists upon acting as companion
to his wife. In many ways, and under particu-
larly stagey circumstances, the vindictive Spaniard
causes trouble. She is the sin that finds the gentle-
man out, the blight which settles upon his respect-
able life, the black chicken that comes home to
roost. Having made her ex-lover sorry for him-
self, suspicious of his virtuous wife, and sick of
everything, the intruder is suddenly brought to
see the error of her vengeance. A good young
man lectures Esther into a penitent state, and she
would probably enter a nunnery if the worried
husband didn’t shoot her in mistake for the good
young man. After which regrettable error the
husband rushes into the next room, and is heard
slaughtering himself with firearms as the curtain
drops.
* • •
“Esther Sandraz” is a good acting plav,
and a difficult play to suit with a company.
Thoroughly well acted in all parts, it might prove
a big Success, but neither this nor any other Dacre
production will flourish much under the present
system of treatment. Frank Cates is out of his
depth again, and one of the imported ladies seems
much too weak to give him any assistance.
Arthur Pacre is excellent in curt remarks and
minor details. Sometimes his mouthing of
passionate speeches, or his London Journal
legs, cannot be commended. As soon as Mr
D. strives hard for effect he becomes highly
artificial and ineffective. Fortunately the
story finds a cheerful little place for William
Elton, and that valuable comedian fills it happiiy .
also Miss Kate Bishop, in a grey wig and a minor
capacity, is a welcome addition to the company.
The performance of Miss Amy Roselle is the
work of a really great artist who has found a
strong subject. Esther Sandraz, a melodramatic
creation, seems a natural, unaffected woman on
the Bijou stage. The audience allowed itself
to te considerably carried away by Esther’s
feelings on S iturday night, and on one occasion a
bookmaker was detected in the act of stealing his
h’apron to his h’eye.

for VVednesday night and Horace Wheatley’s
benefit at the Princess’s, the manner of the adver-

Flirtation ’’ is no doubt running for three conse-
cutive nights if it doesn’t fall flat at its first
attempt. The Bulletin has learned to put no
blind trust in American comedies, with or without
music, and although there are but two extra dates
to fill with MTntyre aforesaid, it isn’t safe to
reckon on MTntyre appearing more than once.
On the other hand, this bold experiment may
answer remarkably well. A definite change of
bill happens on Saturday. “The New Boy ”
(from Sydney) is to usher in a new series of Living
Pictures, and Melbourne looks forward to the
occasion with a sparkling eye.

Miss MAY CUMMINS, Complainant in the
Sydney Military Scandal Case.
Re the Dancing Gearl’s charity entertain-
ments, at Melbourne Bijou, it is asserted that the
“ expenses ” were abnormally heavy. The local
Herald says most emphatically that from the
business aspect of the affair the arrange-
ments were entirely unsatisfactory.” The
same ridiculous journal shrieks, “inasmuch
as the entertainments were advertised as in aid of
charities, and the public had been attracted by
such advts., the financial result was surely a
matter of public importance.” The truth about
those three awful performances at the Bijou is as
follows : An earl who, on his own statement, had a
mad desire to act, and couldn’t presume to act on
his own merits, was easily persuaded to act on the
merits of charity.
• # *
The manager who made arrangements
with the verdant nobleman had the theatre’s in-
terests at heart—“charity” was only the catch-
word, as far as he was concerned. The earl had
his own gratification at heart— “ charity ” was his
excuse. The public (snobs for the most part)
paid their money to gaze at the performing
earl, and the word “charity” didn’t draw
sixpence to the house. Consequently the receipts
are of no public importance at all! Entertain-
ments, said to be in aid of charities, did actually
aid those charities to the extent of .£BO or .£lOO
apiece. If they aided anything else over and
above the charities, it is no business of the public
that went to see the Gear], expecting to witness
a bad amateur performance, and yet had not the
pluck to laugh at his lordship.

Musician Harry A. Nathan, lately married to a daughter
of Aid. John Taylor, of Sydney, is grandson of Isaac
Nathan (author of the song, “ Why are you wandering
here, I pray,” and composer of many of the musical
settings of Byron’s Hebrew Melodies), who came to Sydney
in ’4l, and had amongst his pupils many of the leading
artists of his day. He was killed on the old Pitt-street
tramway in July, 1863.

There is a disposition to give Marie Elster her singing,
due now that she is going “home.” The buxom soprano’s
benefit at Melb. Royal will not happen before April 9, but
already a crowded house seems a certainty. The Vic.
Public Service intends to make the occasion a gallery-
night, so perhaps the University boys will follow suit by
engaging the family-circle.
A little Melb. weekly essayed to treat the Dancing
Gearl’s complaint with criticism and grease mixed, but
they wouldn’t mix. “As an actor, the Earl of Yarmouth
shows no skill, even for an amateur. That he has talent
is easily discernible from the earnestness with which he
throws himself into his work, and endeavors to forget his
own individuality in that of the character.” Histrionic
talent, in an earl, is expressed by his earnest manner of
accomplishing an awful failure.
At a Melb. benefit, the other night, one self-sacrificing
artist faced the audience on a pair of crutches, and recited
under the trying circumstance of a leg in a sling.
London Sketch says of Gov. Robinson’s opera, “ Preda-
toros,” that “ many a light opera has had a long run in
London that has not had so much to recommend it as this
work." Yes, and many an opera has had a precious short
run that has had infinitely more to recommend it. Nothing
can make Robinson’s affair aught but weak. It may
make a splash because a Governor wrote it, but certainly
not on its own merits.
Marie Luella and Lucy Fraser, who boast by far the
shapeliest figures on Melb. Princess’s stage, wage a rivalry
of limbs in the regards of the “Johnnies.” Lacy has
more calf, but the Marie wins by a thigh. Dispositions
differ. Luella moves over the boards with stately
melancholy, Fraser is mercurial. They have been dubbed
respectively “ Hippy ” and “Happy.”
Melbourne’s latest fad is the merry-go-round, of which
there are several now running at suburban watering-
places. They are fairly rushed by giddy society girls, and
differ from the old styles, as the horses rock up and down.
In this is said to lie the charm, jockeyesses imagining
themselves bringing home a Cup winner. They cost
money to build, but the proprietor has already made a
pile, and a co. is being formed to purchase one now in
course of construction.

Conjuror Maskelyne mentions that spiritualism is a
hopelessly insolvent business in America. The last of the
Davenport Brothers peiformed the rope-1 rick at Washing-
ton lately, and after a week bis cabinet was seized by the
landlord. Dr. Slade, the prosperous spook-raiser of 20
years ago, is a pauper lunatic, knocking his head against
the whitewashed wall of a workhouse. He was a wealthy
man when Maskelyne exposed the impudence of his frauds
to a London jury.
