A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat tahe blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.
Plimmer’e dramatic _oo., touring N.S.W. back-blocks
gome time ago, didn't bring back much coin ; but they
brought back a new bush epithet, which is better. Two
of the ladies turned into bed in their canvas-partitioned
room one nlght,and overheard the following conversation
b tween two bushmen next door : “Been to the play?”
“Yes.” “ What dii you think of the girls?” “ Oh, not
too stink." “ Well, good night." “G'-night.” In the
morning the ladies, much impressed, made enquiries, and
learned that they were classed as not too bad, decent
enough, pretty tolerable.
Bush strollers are all “at Poverty Point’’—all struggling
to make an honest living out of the public—all bound in
links of brotherhood. An “advance” recentlv struck a
N.S.W. bush-town three days before the races. A man
eyed him curiously as he came out on the verandah
after tea, then walked up and spoke. “Here for the
races?" “Yes.” “ What’s your line?" “I’m repre-
senting So-and-So.’s Grand Comedy and Dramatic Co.”
“Oh, ay. I’m three-card myself. Come and have a
drink."
Herr Von der Mehden, first of Ohiarini’s, then of the
Faust Family, now of Fitzgerald Bros., is the “ power-
fullest” cornet player in Australia and withal the sweetest.
His range is from a pianissimo as fine as the squeak of a
mouse to a fortissimo as loud as a tornado or an outdoor
political speech. Born in Hamburg, he started as a
drummer-boy in a military academy and learned to play
the drum and speak the truth, lie plays all band instru-
ments, violin and piano. He married one of the charming
Faust girls. He has been a big attraction with his baud
during Fitzgerald’s Maoriland tour.
Old Billy Jones, of Fitzgerald's circus—he was a veteran
even in the days of Burton—was apprenticed to St. Leon
and made his first appearance at Sofala. The circus-tent
consisted of side vvalls only, and the lights were made of
fish-tins filled with mud and some fat and rags in the
centre. These were stuck around outside the ring. The
show was carried round on packhorses. Billy Jones’ first
appearance was when he was “ carried ” round by a rider
standing up on a horse. The rider had tights, but Billy
was dressed in a trousers and shirt and was barefooted. A
large sum of money was thrown into the ring as a reward
for his pluck by the diggers of the Turon. Later on, in
Melbourne, £lOOO was offered to St. Leon for the hoy and
polity by a speculator. St. Leon replied: “No, we
haven’t got to slavery out here yet.” Billy Jones is now
53, and can still carry a very large corporation over in a
neat back somersault. He married at 52. He is the
Parkes of Australian circus-life.

For the first time since the hideous and strictly
indecent crinoline went out of fashion, an actress has
appeared in a London sassiety play wearing hoops. She
represented a country dame of the 1780 period, but not
for long, because the play, “Guy Domville,” was such a
black frost that it helped to put ice on the Thames. It
seems that when leading man George Alexander said, “ I
am the last of the Domvilles,” a disgusted first-nighter
cried, “Thank Gawd for thatl" from the gallery. An-
other proof that dramatists are born, not made; for
Henry James’ novels are full of delicate high comedy,
which seems only to need concentration for the stage. | |

Dear Bulletin, That sraall-keg-of-
smuggled-Jamaica dodge was played very suc-
cessfully in Melbourne, but with this difference —
you could tap it in any part and the nectar would
flow. The secret was a large bladder put in, and
the neck kept outside the bung-hole whilst it was
Ailed with water, tied and dropped; the same
with a second or third or fourth, if necessary.
Some spirit was then poured in, and buyers
sought. As the general public delight in “having”
the Government, the “smuggler’s” success was
phenomenal.— Expert.
The more modern luxuries and necessities
of civilised life are taking permanent root in Mel-
bourne, and among the cultured classes are
beco riing rapidly adopted. Face massage is really
a necessity ; it cannot be properly styled a luxury.
Massage is really the perfection of cleanliness—
penetrating the tissues of the skin and removing
all blemishes and defects. The rooms of the
•Parisian Toilet Company, 274 Coll ins-street, Mel-
bourne, afford an admirable exposition of the
massage processes ; and the same proprietors con-
duct the new system of German vapour and
electric baths, a “ new science of healing,” which
has proved so effectual in the treatment of
sciatica, lung, liver and kidney complaints, &c.
Operators of both sexes are provided at this excel-
lent establishment.

* * * *
Britain’s trade methods, says Wilson of the
Investors’ Review, consist mostly in selling goods to
foreign countries and lending the purchasers the money
to pay for them. Britain’s trade booms most in the
years when she lends most money abroad, and the biggest
boom is always with the countries which did the biggest
borrow.
The Sydney Permanent Freehold Land and
Building Co., reconstructed in 1891, pro pus s 1,0 recon-
struct some more. The half-year's deposit interest, fall-
insj due on 24th March, is to lie paid in six half-yearly
instalments, the first instalmnt falling due on 24th
Sept., 95, and the last in March, ’9B. Any person
who has £lOO on deposit,' at 5 per cent., will
thus receive Bs. 4d. each half-year for the next
three years. Depositors are also to be asked to
forego all of the next two years’ interest, and afterwards
to accept 2| instead of 5 per cent, in full of all claims.
At some date in the dim future, when all the co.’s mort-
gages have been paid off, it proposes to start liquidating
its deposits by instalments, parable at no defined
intervals, of Is. in the £. The hoard acknowledges regret-
fully that all its reserve and contingency funds have
been swallowed up, that the income shows a dimiirntion
of £4579 ns compared with last year, and that there is a
heavy debit balance (some £9000) with which to start the
new year. The co. owes £154,000 to depositors, so the
two years’ interest it proposes to omit paying is just
£15,400, and the reduction to 2j per cent, will mean a
saving of £3850 annually.

In Great Britain every joint-stock co. has to file
an annual statement—accessible to any one on payment
of Is.—showing thepaid-up capital, the number of shares
held by each shareholder, and the calls paid or in arrear
by each individual holder. Some such law is badly wanted
in Australasia. When a director makes a “rallying
speech” to the shareholders of a shaky affair they waiitto
be able to find out whether he is selling his shares, and is
simply trying to hold the concern together long enough
to prevent him being put on a “ B ’’ list of contribmo ies.
They also want to know whether the directors pay up their
own calls, whether they hold the requisite number of
shares to qualify them to sit on the hoard—which they
often forget to do, and to be able to gauge the solidity of
the concern by the unanimity with which the board is
trying to get out. Only when the English law is applied
in Australia it must be enacted that payment of c dls fry
uncollected cheque, or by pro. note, or by overdraft, or
by the co. discounting a hill for the shareholder, do sn’t
count. Unless it is specially stated that the influential
director has paid his calls in cash, that personage will still
find a way of getting out.
• * * »
Moreton Frewen, the apostle of bi-metallism, is
not logical. In Melbourne, before the Vic. Banking Com-
mission, he advocated a State issue of notes payable in
silver, but objected to a State Bank, because “ a State
issue of notes, unsecured, inflated prices, increased im-
ports, and drove gold abroad.” The notes issued by a
State Bank would do that if they had a forced circulation,
but as it is not proposed to give them a forced circulation
they wouldn’t. Also, if a note which is secured by the
State’s promise to pay gold for it worth 20s. to the sove-
reign is going to, depreciate in this dreadful manner,
what is going to prevent the depreciation of a note which
is secured by the State’s equally reliable, or equally un-
reliable, promise to pay it in silver worth only 4d. or sd.
in the shilling?

Dear Bulletin, —Hundreds of Bulletin
readers in Victoria will sniff the musty odor of the
“Early Days” Trade Protection Society men in your
correspondent, “J.8.C.” Methinks I see that dear old
“J.8.C.” munching his stump of a penholder whilst
ladling out to subscribers his threadbare formulas, “fair
risk,” “no good,” “use caution,” &c. That sort of
thing, aided by the owl-like and sapient look he assumed,
used to go down, seven or eight years ago, and he, poor
soul, imagines that Melbourne business-men of to-day are
to be humbugged in the same way. In his time, one
clerk, at £1 a week, used to potter arou d town
with one or two enquiries, and return, after seeing, per-
haps, two or three men, with a report “fair risk” for so
and so—which with “ J.8.C.” used to hold for ever. Nowa-
days, the live enquiry offices have half-a-dozen bright men
apiec l w fl o daily visit every merchant, get the amount of
trader’s indebtedness all round, and with the knowledge as
to what the trader’s capital is and the credits he hasopened,
regulate further credits accordingly. On “J.8.0.’5” old
system (as he well knows) many rocky men did establish
£6OO credit on an original £5O recommendation, hut it
can't be done now! So far as go the “lists J.8.C.”
refers to, nearly every big firm does not read them, but
relies on the information received from the society con-
taining all the details such lists profess to afford. Had
“J.8.C.” riot been buried alive for the last few years he
would find that the scanty details he used to give sub-
scribers, and on which he made money, would at the pre-
sent day be ridiculed. He would be paralysed to see the
able and deep reports which the mo lern information-
office supplies for about half the money he used to get for
comparative guess-work.— Vosnibara.

Editor Bulletin, —The Chinese furniture--makeF
when he sells his biz., sells the name, but does not inform
the firms he deals with of the transaction ; he simply
wends his way to China with the price in gold in his
pocket. The Trade Protection Societies give the usual
reply to enquiries: “Established so many years, and
always found good for trade requirements,” so the new
man extends his credit, and finally sells every stick, and
goes bung; his creditors finding then they have been deal-
ing with Ah Meow instead of Ah Cat, who is plying his
chopsticks ’neath the bamboo in his native clime.—James
Belson, Svdney.

Tiger Inglis, M.P., of Sydney, must be doing
pretty well in England. Not, only has he practically
floated the Sydney Harbor (Cremorne) Colliery Co. for
£500,000, of which £325,000 was underwritten last week,
but he seems to have had his finger in several rather pro-
fitable Westralian pies. Tiger is taking a big contract—
that of raising coal from the bottom of a half-mile
straight sh ift—and it’s to be hoped he will succeed in
making it pay. By the way, when Sydney is able to tip
coal from the pit’s mouth into the ship’s bunkers New-
castle will be a “back number.”
* * * *
Small lots of jewellery and other valuable goods
sent from Sydney to North Queensland don’t pay duty, as
a rule. One or two obliging firms in Brisbane supply in-
voices showing that the goods come from that port and
are consequently not imports from beyond the province.
The dealer often carries them inhis personal luggage, and
he and the invoice between them explain everything.
* » * »
Just now, when openings for the “profitable
investment of capital" are so badly wanted, somebody
ought to start a Public Crematorium Co. in Melbourne
and Sydney. . Fees from £lO for a high-class cremation,
down to £1 for the plainest kind, would yield a handsome
profit, and the business, though it would be small at first,
would be bound to grow to large dimensions.
* • • •
A Sydney money-lender who advanced .£lOO to
a well-connected visitor from Northern Europe, now
pesters the local consul to collect the principal and
interest, as the visitor went “home” without giving
notice. “Ze principal,” said the consul, in an interview
last week, “is right enough provided ze gentleman has
independent means, but ze interest ! Mein himmel, no I
Mein country would put you in gaol for such charges. I
vont forward ze papers unless you strike dat out. Mein
Government vould say Australia was not suitable for a
civilised consul if it saw dese figures. Strike dem out,
strike dem out 1" The interest was only 100 per cent.,
which, from an Australian money-lender’s point of view, is
exceedingly moderate.
* * « •
The Sydney branch of a big foreign bank has
brought its clerks back to work more than once on recent
Sundays. The Sunday before last nine clerks were at
work in the morning, and five or six in the afternoon.
Yet the general manager is a great stickler for Sabbath
observance, and, although several of the clerks who have
been working on Sundays “graft” on week-nights until
10 o’clock, that manager would probably hold up
his hands in horror if he heard of them having a Sunday
picnic down the harbour by w.ay of a freshener. For, ye
ken, Sawbath jaunts are avoidable affairs, but Sawbath
work is sometimes an imperative necessity.
♦ • • *
The experiences of Melb. pubs, just now must
be simply awful. According to one leading honiface
there are only about half-a-dozen hotels legitimately
paying their way. Most of the remainder rely upon ques-
tionable practices, such as Sunday-trading, liquor-
adulteration, &c., to pull them through. Tne residue are
content with remaining ruined barnacles of more or less
rocky breweries.

Building and Lana Investment Association, Limited, a
concern which can’t remove to cheaper premises owing
to the impossibility of getting its initials down thes'air,
reports that, despite much hard economy, it has made a
loss of £lB2B during the year, and that it sees its way to
make further losses of t’17,724. The association recon-
structed last year, and got ten years to pay up, all
deposits to hear 5 per cent, interest. Only three short
years ago the Sydney and Suburban was an imposing con-
cern with £lOO,OOO paid-up capital and reserves. Now a
few more such years as ’94 may possibly make it a matter
of some difficulty to pay even the 5 per cent, on the
reconstructed deposits.

Editor Bulletin,—Under date of Feb. 23rd
you publish a leader in which you stigmatise Belgian
spirit as “a vile compound.” As the representatives of
one of the two disHllers who alone export spirit from
Belgium (namely, Mr. Louis Meeus, whose brand, the
“ Key and Wheat Sheaf,” is well-known, while the other,
the “Bell Brand” of Van den Berg, is also highly
esteemed), we must ask you to correct your statement.
The Geneva mentioned above is the only Belgian spirit
exported to N.S. Wales, and, although the original cost of
distilling does not exceed Is. per gallon, the same as the
Dutch Geneva, sold all over the world, yet the spirit is
distilled from good wheat, barley', and rye, and is
absolutely pure and vvho'esome. The case.of 4 gal. proof is
sold actually in bond, at Sydney, for9s. 6d., yet no one can
say that it is either impure spirit or in any way injurious
to public health. We must ask you, in all fairness, to
publish this letter to disabuse the public on this matter.—
Believe us, dear sir, yours very truly, Jules Renaed&
Co.

At the annual meeting of the National Building
Land and Investment Co., of Sydney, the chairman
announced that he had “strong hopes ” that the deposi-
tors would ultimately be settled with in full, but the
National “could not go on paying interest,” and “it
would only he by the exercise of clemency on the part of
the depositors that it omil I hope to pull through and pay
20s. in the £,” Apparently, the National B.L. and I. Co.
will also have to reconstruct some more, and, seemingly,
on a prettyhard basis for its creditors. The co. is only a
shadow of its form;r self, and is still shrinking visibly.
* • » •
The Industrial and Provident Building and In-
vestment Society, of Sydney, manages to pay interest to
depositors and a 7 percent, div. to shareholders, and ends
the year with £l7 less than it had 12 manths ago. This
wouldn’t have been reckoned a very brilliant achievement
eight years ago, but in these times, when the haggard
figure of depression is abroad, it is little short of mar-
vellous. The Industrial and Provident was one of the few
societies which kept clear of the land-jobbing madness;
hence its survival amid the ruins of so many competitors.

* * * •
Certain English and German firms now supply
in quantities blank invoice forms for all the leading ex-
port firms of the world, and any invoice forms not kept in
stock are made to order. They are exact duplicates of
the real article, and are very cheap. Thus, when the im-
porter gets his genuine invoice he can himself make out
the bogus one at reduced rates for the Customs. This
saves a lot of trouble. Some exporting firms don’t care
to supply bogus invoices. Sometimes the bogus invoice
goes astray, and even if it doesn’t there are too many
people in the secret. Also, the man who makes out a
bogus invoice in England dm sn’t know how to make it
suit the situation. Sometimes a whole invoice which is
judiciously “faked” to save £3OO in duty is useless
because it contains one small line of goods at an absurdly
low rate, and another firm has been bowled out the week
before passing sham invoices for the same class of goods,
and the Customs authorities are on the look-out accord-
ingly. Always make out your own bogus invoice.
• * • •
It is well-known to Vic. shipping-clerks that in
passing entries, to use one name for a certain article
means no duty, but to use the other equally correct name
means 40 per cent. duty.
* » • *
A salesman of a wholesale firm, whose resigna-
tion was suggested and accepted, armed himself with gilt-
edged lettens of introduction and recommendations from
bankers and merchants, and took a trip ’Ome in search of
agencies. By these means he secured the sole agencies of
some twelve firms at a salary of £5O each per annum and
commission on sales —the agreement being, in nearly all
eases, for five years. As his office expenses are under
£lOO a year, and biz. is at zero, he is able to take his ease
and wear purple and fine linen.

Forty years ago a cettain auction-room in
George-street, Sydney, was rented at £6OO per annum.
To-day its annual rent is £3OO. But the rent of a certain
chemist’s shop in the same street was £4OO a year in ’56,
and is £6OO a year now. The man who establishes a
business generally has to share it with his landlord.

The first raid ever made by the Vic. railway
authorities upon passengers travelling on “Croak "tickets
occurred in ’B7, whew 380 culprits were bagged in one act.
Only two, however, were prosecuted. As to yearly rail
way tickets, it is nearly impossible to detect fraud at
once, inasmuch as the tickets are made of the same metal
every year and the inscription on them is very small.
Why don’t all the departments change the metal (or the
shape) of the passes every year and {re Parliamentary
passes) within a week after every general ebetion?
• * • •
The Garangula (N.S.W.) goldfield offers some
more beautiful results of private ownership in mineral
land. The owners now d -maud from the men 10s. per
month pe,r man in advance, so that whether the search
for gold is successful or not their profit is secure. In
addition, they are demanding hack-rent alleged to be due,
and threaten if it is not paid to treat the miners as
trespassers. Apparently the recently-passed Mining on
Private Property Act cannot help the men, who have no
option but to pay—or go. Why on earth the minerals
under the land should he the property of anyone who
merely pays the Crow for its surface-value is a question
that Australian legislatures might set themselves to
answer at once.
* • * *
N.S.W. Mines Minister Smith is now consider-
ing a big leasing dispute between two rival parties, one of
which is understood to represent the powerful Broken
Hill crowd. The disputed lease (for which £lO,OOO cash
and an interest is said to have been given) is somewhere
in the Bathurst region, and appears to be a very valuable
property containing several rich reefs. The tangle arises
out of complications connected with the Mining on Pri-
vate Property Act, and much curiosity is expressed in a
wide circle concerning the Minister's decision.

bushels from 1,740,000 acres.

The eleventh hour of the Turkish Empire
seems to be striking, and Europe will probably
put the Sultan’s clock on another 50 minutes. Yet
that same eleventh hour has been striking regu-
larly for 200 years, and still the Othmans hang out
at Stamboul.
At a meeting of the creditors in the estate
of Crawford, King and Co., the great outfitters
and drapers of Melb., it was determined that the
realising sale should be continued for a month
longer at a greater sacrifice than ever. Residents
in the bush, and in the remoter country towns,
who do not often visit the cities, are invited by
advertisement elsewhere to do their shopping with
Crawford, King and Co. by post.

Face-massage is the latest fashionable
vogue in Melbourne, and Mr. H. Westall Guest,
the well-known Bourke street chemist, has laid
himself out to adequately fulfil'his mission as the
apostle of the system. The processes of treatment
are agreeable in the extreme, and wrinkles, erows-
feet, blemishes and the other outward and visible
signs of change and decay vanish before the touch
of the masseur. It is almost needless to explain
that this is science—not quackery. Mr. Guest
has already established a considerable reputation
for his many artistic specialties of the toilet.

APPEARANCES ARE DECEITFUL.
When the Duchess of M —th saw for the first
time the celebrated author of “ Vanity Fair,” who
was a very stout man, she exclaimed, “ How
deceitful appearances are; I would have thought
Mr. Thackeray thin, whose satires are so sharp.”
This remark on the inconsistency of his looks
with his writings being reported to W.M.T., he
shrugged his shoulders and said, with a smile,
“ Her ladyship ought to know, however, that my
weight is no joke when I sit on anybody. ” Appear-
ances are deceitful, arid to judge from them alone
is sometimes a mistake. Frossard’s Cavour Cigars
are the best illustration of the old saying. Rough,
in appearance and shape, they are a mild and most
delicate aromatic smoke. Packets of eight soldi
for one shilling.

A swindler has been victimising trades-
people by passing forged cheques purporting to be
signed by a very prominent titled landholder. As
the signatures are in a wretched hand, and
meander like the pulse of a “ moderate ” drinker,
nobody had the least hesitation in cashing them.

The Late ex-Justice MANNING (N.S. Wales).
The informer in the sensational abortion-
cases, recently tried in Adelaide, was formerly
the owner of a big plantation in India, and also
Practised as a lawyer. He and his brother went
o Melbourne to inherit a fortune left by a relative
in boom-time, whioh eventually paid them about
a shilling in the pound.

_ Since Responsible Government Mr. Justice
Simpson is but the second who has ascended
N.S.W, Supreme Court Bench direct from the
Attorney-Generalship. Judge Wise was the other.
Judge Windeyer was Attorney-General when he
accepted a temporary Judgeship, and Judge Innes
was promoted from the Ministry of Justice.
Vic. Chief Justice Madden believes in
walking, and has long, for exercise sake, habitually
alighted at a railway-station several miles from
his home. This custom once directly saved his
life. The train so disastrously wrecked at
Windsor was the one he left at South Yarra, a
mile and a half from the locality of the disaster.
“Mick” Simmons, the Sydney tobacco-
merchant and alderman, left <£33,680 all made in
a few years. He began life as a comic-singer at
the old Scandinavian Music Hall, Castlereagh-
street, now the site of the Tivoli Theatre. He
was a man of vast energy and much public spirit,
andhe possessed the brightest pair of eyes ever
set in a human head.
Prior Vaughan, a brother of the late R.C.
Archbishop of Sydney and of the Cardinal Arch-
bishop of Westminster, has arrived in Australia
to explain his scheme “ for the amelioration of the
condition of the masses and the conversion of the
heathen at home”—his home. He came to Adelaide
by the Austral, and, after a fortnight there, passes
on to Melbourne and thence to Sydney.
To Dean Macartney, of Melbourne, who
died notlongago and had thebiggest funeral known
in Victoria for many years, his friends and
admirers decided to erect a memorial at a cost of
Up to date, however, despite urgent
appeals, only about .£lOO has been raised. Mel-
bourne has been stonebroke so long that memorials
can now only be erected on the instalment system.
Parkes went to Kiama (N.S.W.) the other
day to prophesy what the population of Austra-
lasia would total in a few years’ time. Who says
that “experience teaches ? ” In ’BO, Parkes went
to Melb. International Exhibition and prophesied
that, by the end of ’Bl, the population of Aus-
tralasia would be three millions. Butitwasnt.
That was 15 years ago, and the Old Man’s
actuarial bump has been softening ever since.
It is delightful to watch Vic. Chief Justice
Madden on the Bench. He divides his attentions
equally between his moustache and the case before
him. His “mo.” is most delicately waxed-up at
the ends into little curls, which, however, insist
on falling out of the horizontal, and manifest a
distressing tendency to hang down. The great
Chief Justice is therefore obliged to devote half
his judicial energies to keeping his hair in curl.
The death of Mrs. Booth, widow of the
long-deceased Rev. Caleb Booth, in Melb., may be
a reminder of an extraordinary occurrence. A
Melb. morning paper devoted a leading article to
enlarging on an account of how, during the ser-
vice at Northcote Church one Sunday, he came
down in his surplice from the pulpit, took up an
offending dog, and snapped its leg. The affair
proved to be much exaggerated, and only served
to bring out the well-known character of Mr,
Booth for gentleness and courtesy,
Maoriland parodies the facts connected
with the late King Lear’s ante-mortem property
distribution. In this case the victim is a very old
resident, .who made over his property to his wife.
He is now over 70, and has lived at a hotel for 14
years, having been evicted from his palatial man-
sion. He is hale and hearty, eats porridge three
times a day, enjoys circuses, and never “ took on”
about his discomfiture like the lamented Lear did.
He gets an annual allowance of £4OO out of his
estate, which is said to be worth £6OOO a year.
John Stuart Blackie, dead at 85 and a bit,
was no goose, though far from the swan that he
swam out to be. He had a vast, heterogeneous
intellect, akin to some Maoriland hot-springs—
always seething and bubbling, and belching forth
now and again a geyser of Greek or Gaelic not
unmixed with mud. He wrote a great deal, and
nothing memorable ; but he was always a subject
of profound Scotch admiration, which never
heeded the critical laughter that, outside his own
borders, greeted his riotous rhodomontade.
Ex-Justice Manning, Chancellor of Sydney
University, is dead at the age of 84. He was an
Englishman, called to the Bar in 1827, and had
lived in Australia 57 years. He was for five
years a member of the old N.S.W. Legislative
Assembly, and was Attorney-General of the first
Responsible Ministry in 1856. Half-a-century
ago he was offered and declined the resident
Judgeship in Port Phillip, and he could have
been a N.S.W. Supreme Court Judge many
years before he finally accepted the position-
in whioh, not being a lawyer of the first flight,
he was only a moderate success. His lack of
aggressiveness, to say nothing of his absolute
clean-mindedness and scorn of trickery, prevented
him from achieving what is called distinction
in local politics; but, as a kindly and honor-
able gentleman, he was revered by all who
ever knew him. His fine sense of honor caused
him to voluntarily sacrifice himself for years in
the discharge of a pecuniary obligation incurred
by another, and for which he was in no sense
legally liable ; and other grievous burdens not of
his own making he bore uncomplainingly. He
was one of the rare men who grow more liberal
with age; and last year, when all the strength of
Tory capitalism in N.S.W. Legislative Council
was arrayed against the Mining on Private Pro-
perty Bill, his short, ironic speeches on the selfish'
and unwisdom of the wealthy lower orders
did much for the cause of Liberalism and the
working miner.
Wicked North Queenslanders used to say
that Florence O’Driscoll wasn’t born to be drowned
anyway—and he evidently isn’t. Of four others—
Darlot, the N.W. squatter; Duffield, of Bewick
and Moreing, London engineers ; Hardinge, a
mining expert; and Fox, an English syndicate-
agent—concerned in the recent deplorable yacht-
accident at Perth Water, W.A., though all could
swim, not one reached the shore. O’Driscoll
safely got over the distance, and Dr. Fitzgerald,
of Queensland, clung to a plank and was picked
up. At least three of the deaths must be added
to the long tally of Coolgardie.
Mrs. Mott, S. A.’s “oldest woman,”
a colonist of 55 years, really had passed
the century, as the English register proves
her to have been 101 years and two
months old. The ancient dame married twice,
but the second time “incompatibility of temper ”
led to separation, and she resumed her first mate’s
cognomen. She was the mother of 14 children
(eight of whom still live); and as most of them
were as prolific, over 500 descendants, comprised
in five generations, are scattered about Australia.
She retained excellent eyesight to the end, and
for this was indebted to the otherwise trifling cir-
cumstance that she could neither read nor write.
Rev. A. R. Blacket, late of Sydney, and
still more recently of Prahran, Melb., is abandon-
ing a good clerical post to go to Persia as a mis-
sionary. He explains that whereas in Victoria
there is one minister to every thousand inhabi-
tants, there are in Persia tens of thousands
entirely without religious ministrations. He has
also discovered that married men who are not
young are specially wanted for disseminating the
Gospel in Persia—a most mysterious pronounce-
ment. Mr. Blacket was one of a crowd who
visited Carbine’s Melb. Cup meeting to hold a
prayer-symposium on the Flat.
Jack Gardiner, the footballers’ member for
Carlton, was a flourishing institution during the
Gillies period of Vic. politics. He drew an extra
£3OO as whip for the “party”—which included
the whole House, and didn’t want whipping to
vote for new railway lines—and he had as good a
time as any of them. But the young football-
barrackers grew older and wiser, and one sad
election-day Jack Gardiner found himself much
rejected. Now he seems to be in a state of
“extreme necessity,” which is the Minister of
Railways’ excuse for having taken “ poor Jack ”
on as a casual hand, whilst old employes are being
discharged.
M‘KIBBSIN, the N.S. W. bowler.
The January Bookman sheds some light
on the mystery of how the much-boomed Guy
Boothby came to annex the bumptious title of
“the Australian Kipling.” “It was, we believe,
in Melb. that he met Mr. Rudyard Kipling, who
read much of his MS., and thought so highly of
it that he advised him to devote himself to litera-
ture. Mr. Kipling also gave him much good
advice as to the management of his literary wares,
and we have heard Mr. Boothby say that the last
thing Mr. Kipling shouted to him from the deck
of the liner on which he was leaving Australia
was—‘Stick to it and put your trust in Watt,’
The ‘Watt’ is, of course, the well-known literary
agent.”
Rev. Shirley VYaldemar Baker has still
business connections with King George Tubou 11.
and the Tongan Government. The other day a
“white” Government official received a letter
from him, asking why the Tongan Government
were not continuing to send all orders for goods
through him as formerly, at the same time re-
minding said official that he owed his billet to S.B.
The Tongan Government imported goods to the
amount of <£osoo last year, so the commission—
-5 per cent, and pickings—is worth looking after.
Some little friction, it seems, had taken place over
50 cases of Norwegian beer (sent for M.’sP. during
the session), which the rev. gentleman picked up
at auction. The Tongan Parliament’s grog-bill
for its session of three months was ,£4OO.
N.S.W. District Court Judge Forbes, now
75 years of age, has got a year’s leave. Mr. Frank
Rogers, Q. 0., is mentioned as his successor, tem-
porarily and permanently. Mr. Rogers, it will be
remembered, was president of the N.S.W. Land
Court, and resigned because Government declined
to give him Supreme Court judicial status ; he
argued that, though he had no guarantee of the
continued existence of the Land Court, he was
compelled, while its president, to become a mere
land-law specialist and fall out of touch with
ordinary practice. Mr. Rogers, who has the
advantage of never having been a politician, is so
good a lawyer and so universally esteemed a man
that his acceptance of a District Court judgeship
will by no’ means bar his way to the Supreme
Court bench.
George Ash, Premier Kingston’s legal
partner, is making a great name in 4he S.A.
courts. When he gets on to a technical objection
he is simply an inspiration. The other day ha
appeared for a defendant with 26 volumes in his
arms, and followed by a strong youth carrying 23
more. Then he intimated that he had 32 technical

objections to raise to the information, and on each

Last season, Charlton, one of the best-.
Australian judges of cricket, just before resigning:
from the selection committee cf N.S.W. Cricket-.
Association, went up to Mudgee with a Sydney
team, and stood umpire in a match against th.<->
Western Districts. He wanted to find a bowler,,
and he found one in M'Kibbin. The colt only
did moderately, getting four wickets in the firs*
innings and three in the second, but there was
something in his style, and in the way his ball flew
up from the pitch, which caused Charlton to
become his ardent champion. True, he didn't
altogether promise to become an interprovincial,
much less an international, bowler, but he was yet
a better man than the Sydney Club bowlers pre-
sent—W. Richardson, Hume, Sweetnam, and
Pierce. M'Kibbin played early this season with
the combined country team against a Sydney
eleven. The match was a big picnic for Donnan
and Gregory, who fairly hammered the colt’s
bowling, “ quilting ” him for four every time he
pitched the ball up. It was good for the specta-
tors ; it was good for Donnan and Gregory, who
retired each with 201 to his name. But it was
very bad for M‘Kibbin; his chance looked blue.
Still Charlton, Portus and Moses, watching him
in practice, insisted that he would yet do. And,
blessed with their hopes, he has represented
N.S.W. in every big match this season. He didn’t
come off against the English in Sydney, but his,
ill-health at the time was all-sufficient excuse. He,
got five wickets for 98 against them in Brisbane,,
and had five chances missed off his bowling.
M’Kibbin’s record-feat -was his eight for 00’.
against S.A. in the second innings. There is,
nothing succeeds like success, and many peop’e.
fancy that M'Kibbin’s success this season argues,
that he will ri val Turner at his best. But Charlton,,
who knows better, is far less sanguine. “He.
cannot do as much with the ball as Turner at his,
best, and will he learn to place his field as well!
and become as great a tactician?” M‘Kibbir«
bowls his bad ball when he tries to get too much
break on. He bowls mostly off-breaks, but sends
along a real good one at times in a slow leg-break.
And'’ he shoots in a fast, straight ball, which is
very deceptive in the flight. But M‘Kibbin was
“ properly pasted ” by the English in the last
test-match. How sardonically the discarded
Turner must have grinned at Giffen and Blackham,
the godfathers of the new man !

Uniform names for the Australias :

Premier Kingston (S.A.) affirms that he
will accept Australasian federation on the basis of
“one adult, one vote.” He is an advocate of pro-
portional representation in local politics.
Dear Bulletin, —You say S.A. Premier
Kingston is accused by his enemies of a yearning
for an Imperial title. Well, he is going the right
way to get it. Berry blazed the track.—S.S.

Australian elective - Governor agitation. See

Governor shall proceed from the midst of them.”

Sir Henry Loch’s “ friendly recall ” from
South Africa is the more significant now that bis
successor turns out to be the man who was his
immediate predecessor, Sir Hercules Robinson.
It seems to be a case of removing Boch home in
order to restore Robinson to the billet.
An alderman may be fined, as alderman
Collie, of Blayney, N.S.W., lately was, £25 for a
three months’ absence from his council; but a
M'Millan absents himself from Parliament for
eight months, and, instead of being fined, pre-
sumably draws £6 per week all the time !
Much meat-eating seems to conduce to th©
growth of Democratic sentiments. Anyhow, the
butchers of Melbourne are Democrats almost to a
man. At their picnic last week the only M.’sP.
present, .or expected to be present, were Labor
members—Maloney, Prendergast, Winter, Han-
cock, Beazley, Wilkins and Trenwith of Victoria,
and Reid of Queensland.
Our system of passing
acts, and at later sessions bring-
ing in amending acts, is admir-
ably calculated to confine the
knowledge of law to lawyers,
and fog laymen. When an act
requires amendment it should
be repealed and the parts
required embodied in a new
bill, together with the proposed
amendments, so that the bill
would be complete in itself.
The Vic. Railway Com-
mission is alleged to have been
very badly swindled in the
course of some of its investiga-
tions. When it commenced to
explore a new district to find out
if there was enough settlement
to support a line, all the male
population which could raise a
Fiorse to carry it would assemble
at the first stopping-place, to
greet the Commissioners and re-
present the large and influential
local population. Then, while
the officials were driven to the
next township, the influential
population would tear across
country and get ahead of it, and
be there to meet it a second
time. When the same influential
assemblage had met the Com-
missioners six or eight times,
these persons were firmly con-
vinced that such a thickly-
inhabited district ought to have
aline immediately. Sometimes,
it is alleged, not only did all the
able-bodied inhabitants turn up
over and over again, but people
were borrowed from remote
regions for the occasion. A good
many of the unprofitable lines
of Victoria are said to have been
Started by impressing some ob-
tuse official in this fashion.
The other day the shareholders of the
Weekly Herald, S. A. Labor paper, held a
meeting to consider Editor White’s resignation.
The meeting was extremely cordial. Three
minutes from the start Committeeman Proud had
been called a “ liar” several times, and
another committeeman had been described as “ a
sneak,” and as (one word) of uncertain parent-
age. The labor-leagues are hard bosses.
The Sydney Telegraph’s alleged “corres-
pondent ” writes in the Foreign-trade interest :
The return of the Secretary of the Treasury, U.S.A., for
1886 shows: —Population of the United States in gainful
occupation not subject to competition, 16,564,014; popula-
tion of the United States in gainful occupation subject to
competition, 827,184 ; or, in other words, about 96 per
cent, of the population are taxed to benefit or enrich the
other 4 per cent.
Moral : People are only taxed by import duties
when there are imports. In 96 per cent, of
American industries there are no imports. There-
fore there are no Cintom taxes paid. Therefore
the people are taxed because they don’t pay taxes.
The death of Wm. levers, M.L.A. (Vic.),
provides an opening for a pronounced democrat at
South Carlton. levers was a most popular man
personally, and leaves hosts of friends, but he had
no pretensions to political ability. He struggled
and canvassed for years before he won a seat, and
then kept his position through having only weak
men to fight. His opinions were neither numerous
nor clear. During the Turner regime, however,
he gave consistent support to the liberal proposals
of the Government, and was, in consequence, more
popular at the time of his death than at any pre-
vious period. He was one of the most liberal
members of Melbourne City Council.
Dr. Corbett, R.C. Bishop of Sale, Vic., on
that ungodly Education Act :
As long as the new generation of children will be
brought up systematically by the State without the
knowledge of God, as long as the teaching in Christless
schools will be built on materialism, anarchism will
flourish.
Anarchy is the outcome largely of repressive
religion, and wherever the priest or the parson has
his finger deepest in politics there anarchy and
nihilism are most threatening. Victoria, with its
system of secular education, cannot support a
single professing anarchist. How about Spain
and Italy? But there, “’tis if it isn’t if the
praste sez it is.”
Ex-Justice Foster, appointed to N.S.W.
Supreme Court bench, 14th Feb., 1888, at £2600
per annum, had 15 months’ leave on full pay
during his less than seven years’ service, and now
retires at the statutory pension of £lB2B per
annum. The Bulletin does not object to Judges
having largo pensions subject to certain stringent
conditions. On the contrary, it believes in all
Judges and all stipendiary magistrates being well
pensioned ; but, as will be seen above, it is an ex-
pensive business appointing to the S.C. bench
anyone whose ability to serve the regulation 15
years may reasonably be doubted. The country
is now giving ex-Judge Foster £36 a week for the
rest of his life simply because he sat on the bench
for less than six years.
With Hercules Robinson as Governor at
the Gape, there should be no bar to Cecil Rhodes’
schemes, as there are no bounds to his ambition.
It is openly said that Robinson is Rhodes’
nominee, chosen because his age and temperament
make him a suitable alfy for development of the
Great Africa policy—which seems to consist in
looting, for the benefit of English sharks, African
resources all the way from Cape Agulhas to the
Congo, and the establishment of an English
satrapy with Rhodes as satrap, and indiscriminate
millions of “niggers” toiling, after the fashion of
“ the poor Indians,” to pay £10,000,000 per
annum tribute to the Great White Queen. As
Hercules has latterly degenerated into an African
guinea-pig, in companies not unconnected with
Rhodesian manoeuvres, the allies of type and
temperament are still more firmly linked in the
enduring bonds of cash.
“THE BULLETIN’S" AMATEUR ART GALLERY.
“ GOIN’ FOR A DARNSE."
One Fenwick, of Melb. City Council,
objects to the proposed minimum wage on the
ground—as reported by the Age—that “ tlie
workingmen never give Is. or half-a-crown to
charitable purposes; they are regular grab-alls.”
Now, unless Eat Man Fenwick knows all the
workingmen in the world—which he doesn’t—he
can’t truthfully say they never give anything to
charity, therefore it would seem that he says it
without caring whether it is true or not. And,
unless Fenwick is the Almighty—which he isn’t—
he couldn’t tell whether the workingmen, even if
he knew them all, gave or didn’t give to charity.
Therefore, The Bulletin draws Fenwick’s atten-
tion to himself as a foolish and not very scrupulous
bigot, whose statements are calculated to call his
veracity into disrepute. Also, his existence shows
how very little brains are necessary for a city
councillor, so long as he has a large mutton-fist
with which to dab down statements whose crude-
ness and want ol artistic finish would make
Ananias feel ashamed.

The sort of ooze which current political
leaders employ to solve high State problems :
Dibbs : “You junketted in Hobart at the public
expense.” Reid : “You junketted in England to
the tune of £1000.” Dibbs: “ You let Want
away on full salary for two months and a half.”
Reid: “You let Barton away to Vancouver on
full salary for three months.” Parkes : “All
these practices are illegal, unconstitutional, and
deserve the severest censure.” Dibbs and Reid,
facing about together: “ But you were guilty of
the whole of them yourself.”
In the recent Melb. libel-case, Morris v.
Age, little Ur. Maloney, M.L.A., deposed that in
his opinion David Syme is “the mqst powerful
man in the English-speaking world. V The Argus
did not report this—evidently because it was
making too much of David ; the Age also
omitted it —clearly because it made Syme look too
ridiculous. Syme is fidgetty about being dis-
cussed in his own paper. He dislikes personal
publicity, and many a reporter has endured his
frowning displeasure on account of haying men-
tioned his name in a report. But he is right. The
strongest journal would be one coming from no
one knew where—which rose with the sun daily or
was vomited forth by an earthquake at a given
hour every morning. And, if a paper must have
a proprietor, the ideal proprietor would be a man
with no interest, in this world or the next, but his
newspaper—who belonged to no church, club or
society, and who made it his business to know
everybody and yet be known by no one.
There is a possibility of the early retire-
ment of Mr. Justice Manning from N.S.W.
Supreme Court Bench. He is a more recent
appointee than ex-Judge Foster, and has been in
the Bankruptcy Court just about five years. He
is young as Judges go—being little over 40 —but is
in very indifferent health. During the hearing of
the will-case, Perpetual Trustee Co. v. Clarke, in
connection with the man who drew Glenloth in
Tattersall’s sweep a few years .ago, his Honor
took ill, and after the case had been held over for
about a week, it was transferred to Mr. Justice
Owen, and all the previous evidence had to be
either sworn to or reheard, causing much expense
to the litigants. As a barrister, “Charley”
Manning was most at home in Equity Court.
He has made so excellent a Judge that his enforced
retirement—apart from its heavy cost to the
country —would cause great regret.
Robert Louis Stevenson’s portrait of him-
self :
1 am a kind of farthing dip,
Unfriendly to the nose and eyes;
A blue-behinded ape, I skip
Upon the trees of Paradise.
At mankind’s feast I take my place
In solemn, sanctimonious state.
And have the air of saying grace
While I defile the dinner-plate.
I am “ the smiler with the knife,”
The battener upon garbage I
Dear Heaven, with such a rancid life
Were it not better far to die?
Yet. still, about the human pale,
I love to scamper, love to race,
To swing by my irreverent tail
All over the most holy place ;
And when at length, some golden day
The unfailing sportsman, aiming at,
Shall bag me—all the world shall say:
Thank God, and there's an end of that t

has found that prohibition

The Times, it is cabled,
speaking of Australia’s hos-
tility to Asiatic immigrants,
“advises Australia not to
imperil its Oriental trade for
the sake of race prejudices.”
The gospel of trade : “Flood
the country with Chows and
coolies, damn the white
worker, and God bless the
big dealers, for of such is the
Kingdom.”
The opponents of the

movement prophesied that
none of the communities
Would hold together for six
months. Several, however,
joyfully celebrated their first
anniversary doting Feb-
ruary, in which month tlrere
were 1700 persons* healthily
and contentedly living in
eleven settlements on the
Murray. But then the S.A.
system is individualist-co-
operative,; not socialistic-
communistic.
R. 0, Bishop Corbett,
of Gippsland, exhumes cer-
tain words once used by
Wilberforce Stephen, who
fathered the Vic. Education
Bill: “This measure intro-
duces the thin end of the
wedge into the Catholic
body. It will be driven
home, and rend the Catholics
asunder.” Here was a con-
solation for the Anglicans,
who said that Stephen, the
chancellor of their diocese,
was introducing a godless
measure. The sects had
capsized Higinbotham’s Bill,
which made the schoolmaster
a moral teacher.
“ Long ago, as a boy, I was taught to
speak the truth and shame the devil. Nowadays,
you speak the truth and shame the respectable;
which, however, is much the same thing.”—
Richard le Gallienne.
How U.S.A. suffers by Protection. Otto
Von Der Busch, wool expert, of Dalgety’s, ex-
amined by Vic. Tariff Board, answers a question :
“The better class of wools are exported, are they
not?” “It all depends on the demand. The
better classes go to America in consequence of the
higher duty levied in that country.”
Let some N.S. Wales M.P. look up the
circumstances under which an individual who
lately got/two years for embezzling .£lOOO or so,
was released from gaol after serving only 10
months of his time. The sentence appears to
have been thoroughly well-deserved, and the
release has been kept remarkably quiet.
Dear Bulletin, —I worked last harvest
for one of Victoria’s hungry cookies—a God-fear-
ing man. First day we filed in to dinner I noticed
the only things on the table were several bottoms
of ale-bottles, upside-down, filled with salt. The
old man put his hands over his eyes and asked a
blessing on what we were about to receive, and
every soul of ns grunted “Amen.” Then Biddy
lifted up the big pot and threw on the table about
101 b. of boiled potatoes. “Now,” said she,
“ there’s plenty of salt; help yourself, me bhoys.
It’s a foine feed, barrin’the mate.” We struck.
—O.D.R.
Dear Bulletin, —I had a man laid up at
my place a few weeks ago ; he told me he wanted
“a course of medicine.” I replied that I had pills
and offered him some. He smiled- sadly and asked
me how many I had in stock. “Two boxes.”
“ Ah,” said he, “two years ago I took 80 pills
within 24 hours.” Eighty pills! He declined
my paltry contribution of two boxes as rob-
bing me without helping him. He left for
the nearest town where he could revel in pills!
All that was the matter with him was indigestion,
caused by over-smoking and drinking too much
stewed tea. He smoked nearly all night and
drank stewed tea-leaves all day. He wanted pills,
and by, this-time I hope he has had enough.—
Diogenes, Bourke, N.S.W.

"The Vic. Minister of Lands makes some startling disclosures. One mortgagee in possession as he is assumed to be, holds 32 selections, containing 9276 acres,
and owes in rent £1959. Auother is the holder of 26 selections, comprising 5339 acres, and owes £ 981. Another has 14 selections, with an acreage of 3562, and owes
£375. Auother has 21 selectious, comprising 3072 acres, and owes £913; and yet another has 15 selections, with 2386 acres, and owes £735. Are these men the
"poor farmers" for whom sympathy is claimed? They are actualy in possesion of large eslates made up of the homosteade of crushed selectors-crushed mainly by
the excessive intereste extracted from them."-THE AGE
VIC. MORTGAGEE (to Minister Best) : "Speaking for dozens of' poor farmers ’in this locality, I assure you they are not now in a position to pay anything'.'

Duke and Duchess of Madden are pre-
paring for another little burst of royal state in
Melbourne. The Hopetouns leave Government
House for ever at the end of this month, and
Brassey doesn’t steam up his yacht for the voyage
to Victoria until July. There will be more than
five months of Madden sovereignty. For 22
glorious weeks the Chief Justice’s lady will
“lead ” sassiety, and for 154 days her Astrakhan-
collared husband will meet his waxed moustache
on level terms, so to speak ! The illustrious pair
ought to feel happy, and they do.
Lady Hopetoun, for her part, hugs the thought
of getting away from Melbourne. The burden of
dear Lady Loch’s mantle has been almost more
than the diffident Irish colleen could bear. She
started in utter ignorance of what she was
expected to do, and the conviction that her smile
was unsatisfactory and her conversational powers
limited, settled on her mind and impaired her
digestion. At first Hopetoun used to catch his
Countess’s frightened eye, and make faces to the
effect that Hingland expected every Governor’s
wife todoher duty, but this marital pantomime only
embarrassed her the more. Lady H., trying to seem
vivacious, whilst a hundred women had their eyes
on her, was such a sad spectacle that everybody
felt much better wiien she relinquished the
struggle. Nature never intended Lady Hopetoun
to play the hostess. Perhaps Mullins, the
founder of her noble family, was a bashful person
—although retiring pork butchers don’t, as a rule,
achieve much greatness. Anyway, this particular
Governor’s wife, who makes herself very affable to
dumb animals (they can’t talk back), and enjoys
life up-country, is so frightened of “ company ”
that she has often wept, she says, before going
forth to face a dinner-party at. her home by the
smelly Yarra. Wherefore, The Bulletin expects
to hear of the ill-fated countess making her final
escape from Melbourne in the dead of night,
leaving her husband to disperse the mobs of lady
admirers who will come prepared to kiss her by
main force. The Hopetouns have arranged to sail
from Australia at Adelaide, in order to avoid the
ordeal of a long “ demonstration ” on Port Mel-
bourne pier.
# * *
“Rational” dress, The Bulletin is
credibly informed, has got hold of "the women by
night as well as by day, and they are taking to
pyjamas quite easily. Apropos, burglars lately
entered the house of a well-known Melb. musician,
and, while thfe female portion of the family were
huddled pyjama-clad, paterfamilias chased the
midnight marauder in a flowing garment.
• • •
It is said that English
mercantile captains voyaging to
and from this country are
instructed not to ship Austra-
lian boys as apprentices, be-
cause they are too smart and
self-opinionated to make good
seamen. To which The Bul-
letin begs to say—first catch
your boy applying for a briny
billet before you reject his
overtures. An adventurous
Australian juvenile doesn’t
threaten to run away to sea
when he quarrels with the old
man. He talks of going up-
country. If he happens to be
up there already his ambition
is to go down to town. To
sea, no !
9 9 9
An old man has for years
lived on the dead of a certain
sweet-smelling Australian river.
His trade, as stated by him-
self, is “dragging for stiffs.”
It is related of his predecessor
in the business that the wife of
a wealthy man gave him .£1 a
day to drag for poor hubby.
One week went, and a second ;
then the pay stopped and a
substantial reward was offered.
Next morning the old ’un,
looking quite happy, came
down-stream with a corpse in
tow, landed it and got the
money. The papers said the
corpse “ was much decom-
posed.” No wonder —the an-
cient had (somewhat after the
manner of horse-planters) had
it anchored at the stern of his
punt for a fortnight.
# * •
In a fit of insanity, the
other day, anAdelaide barber,
after gashing himself with a
razor, suicided by hanging. A
pleasant reflection for the last
man whom he held by the nose while scraping
his larynx !
« • *
A court-martial is being held in Sydney
upon a giddy hoy in a glaring uniform. He is
charged with “ conduct unbecoming an officer and
a gentleman ” towards a young female, and the
court which is wrestling with the case consists
of the following unexampled mass of gorgeous-
ness :
President: Lieutenant-Colonel Janies Edward Doidge
Taunton, general staff, Assistant Qnarterraaster-General.
Members: Major and Brevet Lieutenant-Colonel Henry
Park Airey, D. 5.0., New South Wales Artillery Regiment,
commanding brigade division, Field Artillery ; Major
Pembroke Lathrop Murray, New South Wales Artillery
Regiment, first garrison division; Major John Henry
Alexander Lee, Corps of Engineers, commanding No. 3
(Submarine Miners) Company ; Captain Haviland Le
Mesurier, New South Wales Artillery Regiment, first
garrison division. Officiating judge advocate : Victor Le
Gay Brereton, a solicitor of the Supreme Court of New
South Wales. Prosecutor: Major Arthur Henry Patrick
Savage, New South Wales Artillery Regiment, Acting
Assistant Adjutant-General. Major Montagu William
Bayly, adjutant 2nd Infantry Regiment, commandant
infantry school of instruction, and Lieutenant Maurice
Alfred Hilliard, adjutant 3rd Infantry Regiment, also
attended for instruction.
The nine members of the court-martial have 31
names among them, and a yard and three-quarters
of title. And all this overwhelming mass of
nomenclature and lace and cocked hat is being
wasted on a case which could be settled
much better by a plain P.M. Every regi-
ment ought to have at least one officer
called B, Smith and nothing more, who
would be kept specially for court-martial pur-
poses. To use Pembroke Lathrop Murray and
Haviland Le Mesurier and James Edward D oidga
Taunton for such a purpose—saying nothing of
Montagu William Bayly or Victor Le Gay Breve-
ton—is awful waste.
9 9 9
There doesn’t seem to be much in the
contention that the regular courts, and not a
court-martial, should have taken cognisance of
the Sydney military scandal. Had the captain
been charged with a criminal offence, no doubt
he should have been tried in the ordinary way i
as his conduct apparently involves only a military
offence, or a ease for civil damages, it is imma-
terial how he is first dealt with. The lady may
please herself. She has still her rights at civil
law, whatever the issue of the court-martial.
• # #
Hard luck for Sydney yachtsmen that
Lord Brassey was not on the cards when N.S.
Wales was vacant. Port Jackson boating men
have ever bewailed the fact that Australia never
had a yachting Governor ; and now that one has
turned up he goes to the Cabbage Garden, and not
to the Beautiful Harbor.
* 9 *
Through the courtesy of a correspondent,
The Bulletin is enabled to print in this issue the
portrait of Mr. Munro Ferguson, M.P. for Leith,
Governor-Designate of South Australia. Mr.
Ferguson is now a Liberal of the advanced order.
In 1886, however, he contested Ross-shire against
the land-league candidate, Hr. Mae.donald, and
got beaten by four to one. He was then a mere
boy, but his cleverness attracted attention and he
was shortly afterwards appointed secretary to
Lord Rosebery, the present British Premier. He
is said to be a rich man, of a bright and emotional
disposition, who would probably make a popular
Governor even in slow-going South Australia.

S.A. Lieut.-Gov. Way has not yet ceased
to repine over the newspaper blunder which robbed
him of the opportunity of prostrating himself
before Prince Henry Joseph of Battenberg. If
the name had only been correctly printed in the
mail-boat list he might have made obeisance
before the brother-in-law of the Queen’s daughter !

The “ Rational ” Woman deserves notoriety, and
she has got it.
One of the leaders of the movement, on her way
back to M.L. per mailboat, concealed her knicker
suit beneath a cloak while on deck, but in her own
cabin threw off the cloak and, like a free woman,
rang for supper. The attendant steward, how-
ever, being a modest man, did not wait for orders,
but, seeing the thin stockinged and knickered legs,
fled incontinently. Another ring, and another

Very few turned to look, the other day,
after a lady, walking down the Block, quietly
smoking a cigarette. The Melb. public are
evidently accepting the inevitable.
• • •
“ I saw a dress-reform woman yesterday
in Princes-street, Dunedin” (M.L.), writes a
Bulletin man. “She had a forbidding coun-
tenance ; her calves were like a coal-heaver’s, her
feet like a policeman’s, and her stride like a sailor
just ashore from a long voyage. All the men
stared at her, the old women glared, and the
young ones giggled. She is either very brave, or
very fond of notoriety. As to the new dress being
a ‘ reform,’ it depends, I should think, upon what
* pre-form ’ was.”
w 9 *
“Gentlemen are admitted as associates”
to the Melb. women’s bicycle club, but are allowed
no say in its management. The latter rule is
superfluous —they couldn’t get a word in edge-
ways.
9 9 9
A Surry Hills (Sydney) milk-vendor (male)
dressed in female bicycle-costume, carries milk
round on a bicycle; while a feminine cyclist
peddles it round the Centennial Park every night,
much to the interest of male wheelers.
* # #
Constable Rackham, of Nunawading, Vic.,
has obtained permission to pursue his duties and
local poultry thieves on a bicycle. Constable
Rackham’s fellow members of the force have no
sympathy with him. It is hard to sleep upon a
bicycle.
* 9 *
Bisbop Beggar, of Kanakaland, tells this
story of his clerical enemy, the Rev. (say) Smith.
Mr. Smith, one day in a Brisbane suburb, absent-
mindedly sat down on a green-ants’ nest, and only
found it out when he had got into the train.
Being alone in the compartment, he took his
trousers off, shook them out of the window, and
—dropped them !
In despair, he retired to a corner of the com-
partment to count his bites. While still thus
engaged the train stopped at a station, and (of
course) a lady got in and advanced cordially to
the rev. gentleman, who had hastily thrown his
mackintosh over his bare legs and sat cowering.
“How do you do, Mr. Smith?” said the lady,
who knew him well. The agonised parson was
just about to remark that he did “nicely, thank
you,” when he found that the ants had got under
the mackintosh, and, instead of the polite greet-
ing he had intended to utter, he emitted a pro-
longed howl.
“Is anything the matter with you, Mr. Smith?”
enquired the alarmed ewe-lamb. The distressed
sky-pilot, busy catching ants under the mackin-
tosh, could only moan feebly in reply. “I believe
you’ve been drinking, Mr. Smith,” said the lady,
severely, “ and —oh !oh ! guard 1” The exclama-
tion was caused by the sudden appearance of a
writhing leg which protruded beyond the shelter
of the mackintosh.
When the train reached another station, the
Rev. Smith gathered the mackintosh round him,
and dashed into the station-master’s room, whence,
as the shades of evening fell, a very subdued
shepherd, attired in the regulation blue-and-gold
of the railway service, emerged and hurried home-
wards.
They say that a fortunate New Woman now
rehearses nightly in the gentle Shepherd’s lost
cords.
• • *
The most notable feature about the case
of Mr. Carey, the unfortunate young English bar-
rister who shot himself in his office in Castle-
reagh-street, Sydney, is that, if he had missed
himself, the bullet—in the opinion of the Austra-
lian Star— would probably have gone through
the ceiling and wounded another lawyer in the
office overhead. The Bulletin once, knew of a
man who missed himself with a pistol-shot and
badly injured a compositor, and there is comfort
in the fact that the only person endangered in the
present case was an attorney.
Melbournian : “On the
Sydney side I read another case
of sacrilege, which renews my
objection to such being made a
crime. I am not sure that
Pentridge does not still contain
a wretched and wronged victim
of the outrageous value set by
a Judge on some cheap utensils
of his own sect.”
# # *
A Melbourne man had
half-a-dozen cottages at South
Melbourne. The right-of-way
to the shanties was 12ft. wide
by 100 ft. long. The tenants
were “ fairly decent pays,” and
the landlord, thinking to im-
prove the property and to make
things more comfortable, re-
cently had the right -of - way
paved with octagonal wooden
blocks. By that act he ruined
the reputations of his tenants
and lost his faith in mankind,
for in less than a week every
block had been dug up and
used for firewood. He remon-
strated, but was silenced with
the remark that “ the d d
fences would go if he didn’t shut
up.”

At the Sydney mortuary
station, Regent- ;t., Redfern, on j
day last week, were several
coaches waiting for the return
of mourners from Rookwood
cemetery. The solemn-looking
drivers were engaged in a game
of euchre ! Subject for Austra-
lian poets: “Lines to the Man
who will play Euchre at my
Funeral.”
An Australian Bishop with a decided
(though unreciprocated) weakness for the fair sex,
had a warm time in a suburban railway carriage,
the other day. Seated next an exceptionally fair
member of his flock, he was worrying her with his
clumsy attentions when the train entered a
tunnel. Sounds of a slight scuffle and an exclama-
tion in a female voice were heard above the rattle
of the train, and the other occupants were
amused, when they emerged into daylight, to find
that his lordship’s fair companion had bolted to
the far end of the oar, while the clerical Lothario
himself—very confused, and with one side of his
face much redder than the oth *r —was busily
arranging his gaiters. A forgotten poet has re-
marked : “If girls won’t kiss you when they see,
don’t try it in the dark.”
• * •
Dear Bulletin, —You state that if one
advertised in Sydney for a solicitor as managing
clerk at .£3 per week he would get 50 applicants.
Some few months ago I advertised for a clerk and
received 178 applications, two from barristers,
several from solicitors, including one from a
stylish attorney apparently in good practice, and
one from a law-clerk who, to my knowledge, some
years previously was apologised to by a Melbourne
firm because they could not see their way clear to
pay him more than =£lo per week. Of all the 187
only one asked as much as .£3 per week, and ho
was not a bona-fide law-clerk. —Yours, Q., Sydney
l/3/’95.

* • •
A Maoriland lady tells some sad tales re
the officers of one of Her Majesty’s ships who are
accused of accepting invitations to dances and
leaving without making any attempt to be even
introduced to the hostess, with failing to dance
with the daughters of the house, and loudly criti-
cising, in their cups, the quality of the liquor
provided. The officers of that ship, say the
Dunedinites, have made a lasting focal impression.

The “Duellists’ ” photographs on view at
Newman’s, Sydney, are excellent, but brains were
badly wanted at the posing. When their man is
hit, the seconds stand gravely by without moving
a finger, and without a vestige of concern. The
doctor is the only one who acts his part. Then
where are the seconds’ swords? and why do they
play to the photographer so obviously when hold-
ing up the dying man in the last scene ?

Another Sydney shark - tragedy—a boy
named James Donald having been so mutilated by
a 14 footer, at Leichhardt, Sydney, while bathing
in 6ft, of water, that he died of shock shortly
afterwards. The recklessness of bathers is some-
thing astounding. The present writer —who
having seen two shark fatalities, is a bit careful
himself—not long ago witnessed the grief of a
dog-owner at the disappearance of his fine retriever
off Double Bay pier. Ten days afterwards he saw
a fine, juicy, six-foot man calmly swimming
over the spot where the dog had been bitten in
two.
The New Woman isn't any
newer than the Pyramids. Neither is
she a “ movement’’—she’s only a
fashion that has been coming and
going ever since Aristophanes satirised
her in “Praxagora.” And, since her
present vogue would long ago have
fizzled out had she not betaken herself
to a quasi-prurient exploitation of
sexual relations, a N.S.W, oratoress,
after standing logic on its head and
cutting all the common-place capers of
the cult, was fain to fall back upon the
good old marriage-question. “Why
shouldn’t the breeding of humans,
she wanted to know, “be as openly
discussed as the breeding of brutes ? v
The Bulletin doesn’t know, any more
than it knows why, upon the’lady’s
reasoning, humans shouldn’t he brutes,
or why indecent physical exposure
should be more reprehensible than tlw
indecent moral exposure which appears
to be the New Woman’s principal
platform stock-in-trade.
Christchurch (M.L.) has a smaller propor-
tion of Chinese than any other Australasian city.
But the fruit-trade of Wellington is monopolised
by the Ch.ow, who ripens bananas and polishes
apples in his own sweet way. An attempt was
lately made by a syndicate of Wellington Chinese
to obtain a footing in Christchurch. A shop was
quietly fitted up and burst into activity one
Saturday morning, but the European fruiterers
at once decided to boycott any auctioneer selling
to the Chows, and the neighboring tenants threa-
tened to move in a body. In the end the landlord
was glad to get rid of his yellow tenant. Thus
the Tartar invasion was repelled. But many of
the fruiterers (who also vend vegetables) surrepti-
tiously buy supplies from the Chinese gardeners.

The two Melbourne morning papers took
very different views of the recent railway-accident
on the Gippsland line. The Argus’ view ran into
24 columns. The Age took a much narrower
view—| col. Which disparity arises about a dif-
ference of opinion in the matter of freights and
fares.

A youth was up before Melb. City Court
the other day for splitting his mother’s head with
a chair. “ He’s a good lad and my sole support,”
pleaded the mother. Three months. He deserved
the sentence, but—does the mother?
* * *
An interesting law-suit, lately raging in
London, concerns a young man who inherited
dboo,ooo, and spent ,£87,000 of ifc the first week in
jewellery (Agra diamond, .£15,000; Hope diamond,
£30,000; seven other diamonds, £30,000, etc.). This
is the sort of heir whose vagaries cause the old
man s spectre to sprint around and tear his hair
o nights. The thing that makes the capitalist
feel as if his death-bed was composed principally
of rocks is the knowledge that his fool of a son
will probably rush out as soon as he is cold and
begin to “ blew ” his pile on diamonds for bar-
maids and girls in tights. Many an old man,
when he looks back on 60 years of thrift, has
cause to wish that he had drunk the money
respectably as fast as it came in.

Melb. churches are turned into green-
groceries for the Harvest Festivals. One has a
haystack, with two parrots on it. Poor church 1
It now lias to scrape in the gutter for half-
pennies with the Poverty Pointers.
A much-retrenched Melb. railway-clerk
says he has “ hit upon a cheap method of existence
without much detriment to his respectability.”
He hires a room (washing included) for 3s.
per week. Meal tickets, buying them at per
dozen, cost him 4d. each. He uses two of
these daily, one for breakfast and one for
dinner ; for lunch he has a long beer, costing 2d.,
and eats all the counter-lunch he can grab. Meals
thus cost him lOd. a day. In all, Bs. lOd. for the
week s board and lodging.

It vyas not generally known that the Duke
of Edinburgh, when first he visited Melbourne,
went ashore in company tvitb Elliot Yorke the
night before he made his public landing, and
visited an old friend of his, the French actress
Madame Celeste, at a Carlton boarding-house,
where the party drank wine and smoked. The
fact is by no means important, but the proof of it
is certainly most amusing—consisting, as it does,
in the circumstance that a few days ago a Vic-
torian lady, who lately succeeded to a very large
sum of money, bought for 30 guineas a gorgeous
glass case, containing three glasses stained with
wine, a bottle still containing a little wine, two
cigar-stumps and a box of matches. On a silver
shield attached to the case is engraved—“ These
glasses, etc., were used by the Duke, etc.’’ The
original owner of this splendid hunk of glory
lately died.
Last hunting-season Lord and Lady Hopetoun
stopped at a wayside Melb. suburban hotel and
took refreshments. That visit may yet cost the
old landlady her reason. The chairs, glasses, cup,
whisky-bottle, etc., are carefully covered up, and
the old girl shows one and all a penny that her
dear ladyship gave to little Tommy.

Dear B deletin', —The reason why
woman prefer the “Chinese market
gardener” to the “European vendor,”
is simply because they perceive more
manliness and candour—more of the
gentleman and infinitely less of the
hog-in the former than in the latter.
“ The cabbagee Chinaman,” as you
term him, is never unkind to a woman;
the “European vendor” sometimes is
—at least, I fancy so. Also, I think
you may be inclined to admit that just
occasionally he really is. The China-
man never turns up his nose at his
former mistress in the street, nor des-
pises her even when every hair in her
head is white and every tooth in her
head lias gone for ever. He is still kind
and gentle to the poor human outcast
and wreck, and has even, to my own
personal knowledge, assisted with
money, store goods, and vegetables
women of precisely this character. Do
the noble “European vendors” act
likewise? It is quite true—only too
true for the unfortunate Chinaman—
that “ women prefer a Chinaman’s
‘ twiddle twaddle ’ to making their pur-
chases in a business-like way with
European vendors” (italics mine).
Women well know that this identical
“ business-like way ” of dealing with
“European vendors ” leads straight up
to the bailiff and the Debtors’Prison.
A bit of harmless chaff and a woman’s
smile, with “ Here’s four shilling and
sixpenny, John! You come next
Saturday, me pay you plenty more,
John,” will send away in a smiling trot
a Chinaman to whom, say, .£6 ss. 4d.
is justly owing; but, by heaven, the
“European vendors” or the District
Court bailiffs are not to be bluffed
in this manner, and women know it. If
you only knew how much money is owing
to the “ Cabbagee Chinaman ” in Sydney and
every town in N.S.W. you would curse me, go
mad, and wear a pigtail. The amount would
astound you, and a fiftieth part of it will never be
paid. Europeans in N.S. VV. are actually living on
Chinamen in the vegetable line. Yours, &c., Ah
Kow, Mudgee, Feb. 17.
