My Dear Moorabinda —
A prominent N.S.W. society family would
seem to be going in for matrimony wholesale—
mamma (relict of a legal man) and her brace of
daughters all being “engaged girls” together. I
think the burning question in that household
ought to be “ Who’s gooseberry ?” Do the
daughters have to cough loudly and turn off the
gas when mamma’s young man shows symptoms of
wanting to occupy the drawing-room until the
witching hour ? And are they heard using mut-
tered language inside the house when their
amorous progenitress makes her good-bye-at-the-
gate longer than usual ? And if all the suitors
ever arrived together who looks after the senti-
mental sextette in the interests of Mrs. Grundy ?
One of the strongest points in favor of what
Shelley called “life’s great cheat’’ (he meant
marriage) is that the connubial bereaved of both
sexes are always so red-hot to go it twice, as the
gamblers say. A wicked Melbournian advertised
in one of the dailies the other day to the effect
that “Rich widower requires faithful wife, looks
no object, but must have great soul”—the advt.
being inserted for whatever fun there might be in
it. He got 76 applications, and 40 came from
widows. Seventy-six great-souled women in
Melbourne? But some of the orthography was
heroic : “ i wish to eorispond with you,” wrote
one*great soul “ i am of English nationality and
a prodistant, i arn a widow this last three years.”
But even these advantages didn’t turn the adver-
tiser’s jest to earnest.
A flighty damsel told me, the other day, as
significant of the mocking state of the marriage-
market just now, that she inserted a similar advt.
in a Sydney paper just for the humor of it, only
her hook was out for a young man, of course. But
slrn didn’t get much for her money. There wasn’t
a bite ! If the market is so shy as all that no
wonder the agony columns in the dailies are full of
hungry parsons’ advts. People who don’t want a
wedding but only howl for a ceremony can get
very tightly tied together for half-a-crown in
Sydney nowadays.
Which reminds me that a youth and maiden
turned up to be welded together, the other day,
by one of these enterprising workers in the Vine-
yard (the grape-juice gets into their noses some-
times !) whose unblushing advts., egging thought-
less people on to matrimony, are on every break-
fast-table. But a hitch occurred in the very be-
ginning. “What is the lady’s name?” unctuously
queried the holy man, and the happy bridegroom
looked bewildered. “ Blessed if I know,” he said
at last—“you see, it’s this way, boss : I only met
her for the first time last night in the dark.”
Now, I wonder if that marriage was made in
Heaven ?
I have discovered a revival in feminine nomen-
clature—there is a modern tendency to resurrect
double-barrelled names. We are only a step off
Maryann, Jemimajane, and those other weird
cognomens which old - time parents rolled
under their tongues like a sweet morsel and were
proud of—just as if a comet’s tail of names sug-
gested affluence to the bearer. The new baptismal
double-bangers are intended to be more elegant
than the old. For example, “Clairellen” is the
newest name out of Smelbourne. It may be
more euphonious than Maryjane—but that is about
all the difference. Both are equally vulgar.
I went to the Haunted Swing, in Pitt-st. (under
the sway of Macmahon), twice last week, and
consider it the completest illusion on tap. There
is really nothing to be nervous about, and it is far
more decorous than the common or garden swing,
where the girls tie handkerchiefs round their
ankles to keep their skirts down. Nobody’s skirt
deviates an inoh from the path of propriety,
though the wearer does feel as if standing on her
head which must rather handicap the show from
a man’s point of view, I should think. It is quite
fashionable to “do” the Haunted Swing. Society
theatre-parties, after having a pleasant chat at the
play, sometimes finish up with a wild whirl right
over, when belledom clutches frantically at its
silken petticoats—though that is not at all neces-
sary.
A bulging audience in a fearful state of war-
paint turned up to sample “The Fatal Card”
(the latest London success), just put on at Sydney
Royal by the up to-date Bland Holt. That
perennial popularity looked about two-and twenty
at the most in a twilight-grey suit with a pink
dickey (surely to the willowy mummer all things
histrionic are possible), although one of the most
absorbing situations in the play occurred when
Mr. Holt, clad simply in a winning smile—was
taking a bath in the stage stream. Methinks rather
too much stress is put upon the horrors of being
found out ablutionising by your best girl—for an
Australian audience at least. Australia is a place
where every headland looks alive with boiled
lobsters on their hind-legs, which prove to be he-
bathera on nearer view. The average Australian
damsdl within oooee of a pond of water is quite
used to her Gussie plain on the half-shell, as it
were.
In this aforesaid bathing-scene (it certainly does
go a bit close to the k-nuekle) pretty Mrs. Holt, in
Australian blue and white, played a rather
“wide” situation very daintily. Hilda Spong
was Margaret Marrable. The bewohus Hilda,
one of the prettiest home-grown (not ’Ome-grown)
actresses in Australia, was distinctly too boister-
ous for a young lady whose papa had such a rush
of furniture to his front verandah. Well-regu-
lated young women are not simooms—neither do
they steer themselves with their elbows. Nothing
is more aggressive than an actress who appears to
consider a fairly roomy stage too small for her to
properly show her action on. Hilda is also
developing fleshily. While the stalwart W. O.
Baker remains in the heroic lead this doesn’t
matter much, but I don’t think a weedier leading
man would look so secure in the same perilous
position.
The dressing in the sympathetically-staged
drama is neat and occasionally gaudy—Virgin
Vivienne, in devilish scarlet and black, looked as
if she got her fashions from Shed, but they suited
the, part. By the way, I was great y re-assured
by Flora Anstead’s treatment of the trousers
which fell upon her out of a clear sky in the third
act. “ Remove those abominations,” she shrieked
—“take away the horrid things!” It is a plea-
sant seivsatien nowadays to hear a woman throw-
ing language like that at the divided garment.
A certain city is pleasantly amused o’ Sundays
just now, I hear, by a “ praste ” who encourages
the attendance at his church of a burlesque co.
performing in the town, although his priestly rival
over the way looks sideways on the painted band
—possibly because the other padre’s tender was
accepted first. But the wider-minded “ father,”
with Tottie and Co. gracefully exhibited near the
organ warbling to full houses every Sabbath day
and the coin tinkling in the colleetion-p'ate, feels
that his is the better part. If every cleric had a
variety co. at his disposal there would, I am sure,
be no need to go about asking why don’t men go
to church.
It has just transpired that the Pope won’t allow
his Fathers O’Flynn (speaking in a generic sense)
to ride a bicycle built for one like the other deno-
minations do, with streaming coat-tails and show-
ing six inches of sock. You see the Pope knows a
thing or two. His church is generally regarded
as a supreme embodiment of worldly wisdom—
or of priestcraft, according to what theological
position you may happen to view the matter from
-rand this Papal ukase ought to set women a-
tlwnking, it seems to me.
For, if it isn’t considered dignified for a priest
to work his legs over the spindle in public, it
certainly cannot be dignified for a woman to go
practising for the tread-mill in public streets —
even if it doesn’t hurt the woman herself it checks
the possible innocence of hobbledehoys here and
there who might think that women tapered off
into mermaids’ tails if they did not seek to
violently prove the contrary. If the New Woman
could only be got to see it, one of the Old Woman’s
very strongest points was that she (in the Queen
of Spain sense) had no legs. Let the New Woman
consult His Eminence Patrick Francis Card.
Moran ere she finally decides to let the world
know that she is supported on forks—l mean
before she definitely adopts the trousers.
But I am wrong to talk of trousers —I don’t
mean because they used to be regarded as a more
or less delicate subject, those benighted days are
past—but because those long leg-bags are not the
goal of my emancipated sisters’ ambition.
Knickerbockers are the things they’re grabbing at,
and have already grabbed in many instances.
There’s not a single instance of a woman yearning
for trousers, simply because they hide their legs as
thoroughly as skirts do, and that’s not the con-
summation the women devoutly wish—for the
New Woman is more badly mashed on man than
evier the Old Woman was. She knows that man is
always keen to criticise the female leg, and as she
is quite as keen that he should have every decent
opportunity of doing so, knickerbockers are the
only wear to achieve the result and yet keep on
the blind side of Mrs. Grundy, Everything is to
be traced to woman’s vanity. I’ll bet you that
Mrs. Brown-Potter won’t be in a hurry for the
Bloomer baglets !
I was sorry to miss the Sydney Fencing Club’s
assault evening (held, however, at 4 p.m., to the
accompaniment of afternoon tea) last Tuesday,
because I am one of the club’s staunchest admirers.
I beHeve in fencing for wotnen. Some day I will
learn to parry and thrust with my parasol and
make deadly feints with my fan, so that the hour
of peril will not find me unprepared. The only
real drawback to fencing is the get-up. Diving
head-gear and pads are both hot and unbecoming,
to my mind. Still, fencing is one of the greatest
figure-improvers among the sports and pastimes
when it’s all over, and is sard to encourage quite a
Junoesque frontal development.
In re the rather boring military scandal, which
drags its slow length along, some of the officers
of the N.S.W. Permanent Artillery are of opinion
that the present malodorous enquiry will about
“kill” the service, so far as Parliament and the
Democratic vote is concerned —though I don’t sup-
pose for a moment that such a trifle will make
society fall away from its epaulette-worship.
Society’s digestion is not weak, from a moral
point of view. One or two glum soldiers, how-
ever, say that pharisaioal skirts have been so hastily
whisked out of their way lately, that it really
seems that the officers’ barracks might as well be
removed to the lazarette.
After all, one can’t expect too much “officer
and gentlemanly ” behaviour from the Australian
service—l think service is a good name for an
organisation chiefly governed by drapers. Nearly
all the Australian War-Ministers and other
legislative militaires are garter-merchants. They
are all horribly bloodthirsty, too—a darksome
state of mind, brought on, 1 suppose, by reach-
ing down the latest designs in blankets from
the top shelf for the old lady who finally goes
out with a reel of cotton, after four hours’ talk.
That sort of thing is enough to make a man
howl for gore, and that’s why so many soft
handed and mild little drapers, all with the
soapy polish of a shop-walker, rear up on their
hind logs on public platforms and breathe blood-
and-thunder.
I have received a copy of the “ Message of the
Wires,” written by Bernard Espinasse, to the
music of George Pack. It is dedicated to Maggie
Moore, who seems to have made a great success
with it in “ Aladdin,” at Melb. Royal. The
“ Message of the Wires” is a very good song of
its class, and one that would score anywhere
where the dot-and-dash science was understood.
The reference to the wife and child dying of cold
and hunger is quite English, you know: and ought
to recommend it to any music-hall artiste on the
look-out for novelties at ’Gone.
From publisher H. J. Samuells (who is alleged
to have spent a couple of thousand in one calendar
year on fostering more or leas Australian musical
genius) comes Ldon Caron’s extremely graceful
song “My Wedding Day.” This is an altogether
attractive burst of melody, and warbled by a
songstress (in bridal robes for choice, if she is a
professional) would “go” in any hemisphere
where they don’t capture their brides with a nullah-
nullah. Tasma Sherwin has sung it with great
success in Sydney, although she didn’t dress the
part.
Prince Josef of Battenberg has departed from
our midst after having done Sydney the honor of
spending all his Australian time in Sunny N.S.W.
Prince Josef and his train of two did the lions
with the assiduity of a Cook’s tourist—he heard
the organ and characterised Wiegand’s perfor-
mance as perfect. He breathed his second-class
royal approval over the veteran ex-Pretnier Parkes
and shook him reverently by the hand with the ex-
pression, “ Now we will die happy.” He also ven-
tured undismayed into Parliament House, and
was doubtless aghast that only one member walked
out backwards—lie was a Government supporter.
The Sydney beauties had reverent worship paid
to them on the Block in Melbourne last week.
Falk’s have just opened a studio there and have
boomed the enterprise by filling the windows with
photographs of pretty women in all sorts of poses,
representing all manner of types of feminine love-
liness. Crowds of admiring youth have, I hear,
been round the window every day, so that it has
been impossible to get near without waiting one’s
turn in patience.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
There has been trouble over the combined team’s
match against the Englishmen at Brisbane, and Iredale,
Gregory, and M'Kibbin are mentioned as having com-
plained of their treatment by J. V. Francis, the promoter.
Francis tells the Brisbane papers that he paid Iredale,
£l5, Gregory £l2 10s., and M'Kibbin £5 Is., besides exes.,
for their services. These three players are, of course,
amateurs ; and the incident sets one wondering anew as
to where amateurism will end. Of course, it is only fair
that they should be reimbursed for their loss of time; but
as soon as payment is made at all the nice distinction
between “loss of lime” and services cannot be main-
tained with any degree of satisfaction.
Theoretically, the line of division is clear
enough. An amateur should not be a penny out of
pocket nor a penny in pocket. The moment he takes a
penny more tnan he would have earned otherwise than at
sport, that moment he becomes a professional. Accord-
ingly, an amateur should charge his losses as a profes-
sional charges his profits - beforehand. Then, as Mr.
Francis says, a promoter (hateful necessity 1) could make
his calculations accordingly.
♦ » » ♦
The luck of the toss has been dead against the
Englishmen in the test-matches. Three times there was
a hard wicket to start upon, and the Australians got the
best of the start. Once it W'as .very wet, and then also
Stoddart guessed head instead of tail, and was ordered
“ in ” by the relentless Giff. Upon the solitary occasion
when Stoddart won the toss he lost all chance of victory
by trying to take advantage of bis good fortune.
On the opening day of the final struggle the
fielding of the Britishers was not more praiseworthy than
the Australians’safe baiting. The ball didn’t go high in
the air from a really rash stroke once during the five
hours’ play.
There was a Black Friday expression on the
face of the multitude at the M.C.C. ground when four
Australian wickets had fallen for less than 150 runs.
When Gregory and Darling started to beat the bounds
with the ball the sea of faces broadened, and Young
Australia was itself again.
Lockwood had to leave the field awhile in the
rubber match, and young Trott again acted in his stead.
Stoddart’s team will always lie remembered for the fact
that they didn’t once meet All Australia without requir-
ing the services of a substitute for somebody at some
period of the game.
• * * *
A most noticeable piece of snobbery at the big
Melb. test-match was disclosed by the operation of the
rule compelling the English professionals to retire to the
pavilion by a different gate to that used by the “gentle-
men.” Why does not some real gentleman player in the
team join the profs, and go in by the same gate by way
of protest ? The team came out to play cricket, not
Sovereign and Subject, or Bank versus File.
* • * •
M‘Kibbin has uncommon flexibility of wrist.
Placing his arm upon a table, with the hand palm-down,
he can, keeping the forearm rigid, twist his hand round
from the wrist until the palm faces upward. This supple-
ness gets many a batsman out; he cannot tell whether
M’Kibbin is going to bowl an off or a leg-break, and
M'Kibbin’s breaks would be bad enough if labelled, for he
often comes back or in a whole foot on a batsman’s wicket.
♦ » * ♦
M'Kibbin got five wickets for 98 against the
English in Brisbane, and had five chances missed off him.
Iredale, who was standing behind the wicket, says that
one leg-break of M'Kiobin’s, completely beating Brown,
touched the leg-bail, making it wobble to and fro ; a hair-
breadth more power and it would have rocked off. As it
was, it went back to its rest in the stump-grooves.
* * * *
About that “throw”(?) of M'Kibbin’s. Phillip-
son, the English umpire, would never no-ball the N.S.W.
bowler. “ It does not look so fair a delivery as Palmer’s,
nor as Peel’s, but it is fair enough in a 1 conscience.” The
Adelaide umpire concurs in this judgment. Many a good
judge thinks that Englishman Richardson’s “throw ”(?)
is more definite y a throw than M'Kibbin’s “throw” (?) is.
* * » •
The best of his fellow-provincial players think
that M'Kibbin is the best bowler on al W'ickets that
N.S.W. possesses, and they most zealous y advocated his
inc.usion in the test-team. M'Kibbin has one strong
opponent—a wonder, whose star, rising suddenly years
ago to meridian height, and staying there longer than
other great ones, is yet s'ow y but surely declining. The
greatest athlete has his day, but Turner dies hard and may
■yet have to be resurrected.
* * * *
E. T. Smith, the S.A. king of cricket, will be
much cut up at having to hand over the Sheffield Shield,
which has been snugly ensconced at his residence at Kent
Town (S.A.) for some months.
* » * *
Adelaide cricketers say that it is not surprising
the S.A, eleven was defeated in Melb. and Sydney. Instead
of encouraging young cricketers, the selection-committee
pick out old stagers like Noel, and ignore consistent
juniors to pick that consistent failure in big matches,
Walter Giffen.
* • * *
The most trying development of these mad
times is the special cricket gossiper who attends test-
matches on behalf of a “great weekly,” and pours a
stream of drivel uudefiled into the next two numbers.
Horse-reporting is poetry compared with cricket gossip,
or “stray notes,” or whatever it may he called, when the
old special persoq gets turned on to do something light
and chatty. He usually starts with the bald announce-
ment that “ It was hot,” after which comes a list of
venerable persons who could, or who thought they could,
remember warmer weather on previous historic occasions.
After a bit the genius gets himself fairly wound up, and
the fountain of small talk plays somewhat as follows ;
“‘l’ve seen worse strokes than that,’ said Dan Grum-
miok, a famous wicket-keeper in the Western district
when the century was younger. Dan was feasting on the
ripe stolid innings of G. GiflVn and exchanging confidences
With tough John Jorrer, whom I remember as a respect-
able cover-point in the early days at Castlemains.
Michael Hump (many’s the time I’ve seen Michael wipe
the perspiration from his kindly lace after a long day’*
leather-hunting on a certain little ground, long since built
over, alas! at Ballarat) had tapped him on the shoulder
as he passed by only ten minutes before the words were
spoken. ‘ Yes, I’ve seen worse strokes than that,' reiter-
ated Dan. Tom Soaker, who hadn’t stirred on his perch
since the resumption of play, looked around with his old-
fashioned''Parramatta smile and cordially endorsed the
terse remark. Tom likewise had seen worse strokes than
that clean, daisy-clipping drive to the boundary, near to
where Peter Maunder, of Adelaide, was leaning over the
rails. 1 What say you? ’ he asked of the‘captain.’ The
captain scratched his head (it still bears the hump he got
from a long drive-one of the best leg drives he ever saw
in his life, the victim is fond of saying) and slowly came
to the conclusion that ‘he didn’t know whit to say.’ ”
Truly test-matches have a lot to answer for.
♦ * * *
Curio : “ I read that Giffen only gets .£lBO a
year as a civil-servant—and what a shame it is he is not
promoted ! One imagines his P.O. superior saying to
him : ‘ Really, Mr. Giffen, you are such a valuable man—
indeed, indispensable—that I hardly see how we can spare
you for this trip to Melbourne ’ —or Sydney, or England,
as the case may be. What does he do? Sort letters?
When does he put in his service ? A goodly sum might
be realised at a shilling a head for seeing him at work."
**• * *
The yarn which reached Sydney about Coning-
ham apologising to Stoddart during the match in Bris-
bane turns out to he cock-and-bull. Bannerman no-balled
Coningham for a throw, and in order to show the in-
fallible Charles the difference between a bowl and a throw
Coningham threw the next ball instead of bowling it.
Then the cricket-liar arose and had his innings.
Hosts of people missed Turner in the Interpro-
vincial match.
How that ’9O team was picked for England is
one of the vexed questions of cricket history. Gregory
says he was picked five weeks before the team left, but
soon after he was put out for Walters or R. M'Leod, and
then at the last, as R. M'Leod couldn’t go, he was finally
given a place. Another historic curiosity of this notorious
“picking” was the selecting of Burns, the Tasmanian, for
wicket-keeping. And Burns, says Gregory, had perhaps
never kept wickets in his life.
Gregory was rather more of a batsman than the
public generally think before he went the first time to
England. The second time he played with the Orientals
he managed to run up 75 runs.
» * » *
A writer in the Clarion (Eng.) had something
rational to say re the results of the first two test-matches.
“The Australian papers are fairly unanimous in the
opinion that Stoddart’s team is the best that has been out
i i Australia. The tall scoring and extraordinary result
of the first test-match have doubtless led the colonials a
bit astray, and made them form conclusions which would
hardly hear analysis. The tour is only half completed,
and for myself I prefer waiting until it is over before in-
dulging too much in comparisons. Our men are doing
7i. ore than the most enthusiastic patriot ever expected
them to do, and doing it in the department which was
considered their weakest. So far, the batting averages me
considerably higher than those of any previous teams,
hut though the Englishmen have come on in the batting,
they’ve sadly deteriorated in the howling line.” The
writ r foresaw that Stoddart and co. would get b aten as
soon as they ceased to score heavily, inasmuch as they
had proved themselves unable to get rid of iheir oppon-
ents cheaply. And he says: “I question whether Aus-
tralian cricket was ever held in as much respect as at the
present time,” which is rather different to the “blow”
cabled out to us as Hinglish expert opinion at the time.

Eleven, London E. News and the Sun kept on cribbing
the P.M. Gazette's special cables until millionaire Aster
got an injunction against them.

* • * *
Five horses started for the V.R.C. St. Leger,
three of them being “ swell ” performers The Harvester,
winner of the V.R.C. Derby ; Bonnie Scotland, winner of
the A.J.C. Derby ; and Oobbitty, winner of the Caulfield
Guineas. This trio finished third, fourth, and fifth behind
that respectable, but hitherto second-class animal, Preston,
and the very third-rate Maroon. Such is classic racing in
Australia.
Opinions differed very much as to The Har-
vester’s inglorious failure in the Leger. Some people
thought he ran like a stale horse, others made him out to
be “ soft” in appearance and style of finishing. Probably
the training tactics adopted with Mr. Sam Cook's obtrusive
favorite, who was prepared for a sale that didn’t come
off, took the heart out of him. One thing is clear, if the
touts were not conspiring to crack up The Harvester they
are absurdly incapable critics. They all agreed in the
yarn that he was looking splendid and galloping bril-
liantly.
The Bulletin made one defensible comment
on the Newmarket Handicap chances—Esau was tipped
as a likely winner if he could “ last out the six furlongs
at top speed—a doubtful point.” Esau didn’t last much
longer than five furlongs. He was leading the field even
after passing the distance-post, but had no say in the
actual finish. Barbarian (who must have won, &c., &c.)

went wrong on the Friday and was scratched, whilst
Bessie M’Oarthy, chosen in these columns as the most
likely of the favorites, finished fifth only. Bessie started
sluggishly and was blocked a good deal, the usual fate of
slow beginners.
By the way, Laundress had pretensions to win
the Newmarket on her “form ” of the previous Saturday.
The writer would have picked her in, say, eleven tries.
Which doesn’t excuse the folly of offering any advice re
the Newmarket, save advice to leave it alone. Book-
makers are not rash men, but the average price at which
the three placed horses started was 85 to 1. Vanitas at
6 to 1, split a couple of 50 to 1 chances.
Fat on’s off-foreleg was very, very shaky after
his pi icky attempt to beat Carnage in ths Essendon
Sta^s.
The Wilson stable started Nada in the Bruns-
wick Stakes with the result of pretty thoroughly mysti-
fying the public, who were thereby “put off” Steadfast
in most cases. On paper the mare, always a public fancy
since she got placed for the Melb. Cup, had the better
chance. Poor public! They provide the added money
which owners race for, yet the necessary object of every
“ stable” is to keep its business as dark as possible. How-
ever, the public are withdrawing their patronage from
the game, and the added money at Flemington is ac-
cordingly growing less every year.

Brilliant, who tied with Theodore for third
place in the V.R.O. Brunswick stakes, ran blindfolded
* * * *
The new woman bookmaker, at Flemington,
lines up with the rest of the crowd to have a “ wet.’’
She wears skirts. Any woman who understood the busi-
ness and could stand chaff could get a huge advt. and a
big start by appearing in knickerbockers.
• • » *
The owner of the Newmarket Handicap winner
is a milk vendor down East Melbourne way, and his coup
was no doubt a godsend to him.
* * • •
You don’t see many obviously bad losers on
Australian courses, but at the Newmarket meeting one
old gentleman, who had hacked Whakawatea, was so
overcome that he lay down on the lawn groaning bitterly
for several minutes after the race.
» ♦ » *
An affecting Carbine poem appears in the
Arous. The noble 'orse is supposed to be singing a fare-
well song to the readers of that Freetrade journal, and
the two last verses run thus:
“ And now, good-bye. To each and all a last and fond
farewell.
Yes, pat me, Hickenbctham, trusty trainer, truest
friend,
And unbuckle this old headstall, while I turn my head
to tell
The people of Australia that I’ll love them to the end.
“ Throw open all the windows, and throw wide the stable
doors,
And let me see my public in this hour of parting dire.
Perhaps some day a eolt may come from England to
these shores,
And in my son they’ll recognise the image of his
sire.”
Poor old Carbine ! He won 36 races in his time, yet there
are bards capable of putting this nauseous stuff into his
mouth.
♦ * • •
Mr. W. R. Wilson, it will be seen by an advt.
on page 20, is disposing of St. Alban’s estate and all his
racehorses per medium of a £125,000 lottery, which, it
may be predicted, will soon fill, as prizes of the sort
offered have an irresistible attraction for the average
Australian citizen.
* • • *
While the tendency in N.S.W. and Victoria is
towards the reduction of horse-racing stakes, in Maori-
land, where the totalisator holds sway, the prizes are ever
on the increase. The N.Z. Cup, which is, after all, but a
mere provincial affair—Auckland, Dunedin and Wellington
all having their cups of nearly equal importance—has
just been raised to £2OOO. Why, with the totalisator at
Randwick, we could have a £BO,OOO Sydney Cup, with
£5OOO divided amongst the runners-up, and a couple of
thousand spielers compelled to work for their living. The
Bulletin holds its hand right up and votes for the
totalisator.
• ♦ • •
The next cable infliction will be the wiring out
of little Paris’ morning gallops.
♦ * * •
Carbine’s new owner is also owner of Petronel
the only other Musket in England.
* * * *
The exportation to Maoriland from Sydney and
Melbourne of trotting-horses which quietly turn-up in
wayback places and take down “ the talent,” has now
become a recognised industry.
* * * ♦ »
The funniest man on a racecourse is the fourth-
rate bookmaker, with not more than £lO in his bag, who
jells 500 to 1, I name it!’ as the horses are coming
down the straight.
* ♦ ♦ ♦
Laundress, who won the Newmarket Handicap,
at 50 to 1, was purchased as a yearling for seven guineas.
The Albion Hotel, the old-time Melb. coaching
and sporting hostelry, has been swept
away. Long ere Victoria had been honeycombed with
non-paying railway lines a former landlady (Mrs. Yewers)
made £13,000 in bar business within ten months. Cleeland
ran the Albion at the time of his Wollomai Cup-win, and
went out of it with a fortune. could do
well anywhere ; when a boy of 15 he ran away to sea, and
turned up ten years later with two ships of his own. The
remainder of his career has been correspondingly lucky.

have originated by this means destroyed over 30,000 acres of grass.

When buying The Bulletin at any wharf
or railway • station, make sure, before paying,
that you get the current issue, as a number of
people have been making money by trading off
ancient copies, on the unsuspecting.
A Sydney Domain orator contends that the
State should open a bureau with a staff of barbers
to shave impecunious unemployed.
A Queensland policeman, when arresting a
man, was “ bashed ” on the jaw with a bar of iron
that knocked out 17 teeth, tore away part of the
gum, and injured the jaw ; yet that constable held
his grip on the man and produced the bar in
court.
Grazing farms are being thrown open in
Bananaland for the encouragement of poor set-
tlers. A grazing farm in a rabbit-infected district
requires to be fenced with wire-netting. This,
with survey-fees for farm of 20,000 acres, will only
cost about .£1000!
“Hallo, Dumpy!” says the honorary
magistrate, “so you’ve been stabbing a man, have
you? I’ll give you six months, Dumpy,” “But,
your Worship,” interposes the C.P.S., “you
can’t—it’s against the statutes.” “Oh, hang the
statutes !” says his Worship • “ let’s have justice.”
Down South, the Government subsidises a
gun and a man to fire it at one o’clock. Some-
times he does so. Recently, this wreck’s liver
told him to go to sleep in the daytime, and he did.
At half-past two or thereabouts he awoke, and
found that the gun hadn’t said anything at the
proper time. He ran for the powder, rammed it
in, and pulled the lanyard, leaving the rammer in
the gun. Then an astonished populace heard the
one o’clock gun explode 90min. after its time, and
the rammer flew out of the bore and demolished
two panels of fencing. The people don’t mind the
90min. delay, and they pass over the damage to
the fencing, but they are now easting about for a
man who will have sense enough not to waste a
rammer without hitting a K.C.M.G.
A Melbourne civil servant, with two or
three different capacities, recently got his capa-
cities badly mixed. He was head of a depart-
ment and also trustee of a public park, the man-
agement of which was vested in his department.
He resigned his trusteeship, and the local inhabi-
tants petitioned the Minister to persuade him to
withdraw his resignation. The Minister sent the
petition to him in his departmental capacity ask-
ing him to interview himself in his ether capacity,
and persuade himself to continue in office, if pos-
sible. Then the residents got up a deputation to
the Minister, and the official was included in his
departmental capacity, his business being to ask
the Minister to use his influence to induce him to
stay on against his own wish. Then the Minister,
despairing, suggested that if he would go and
argue the matter out with himself he might
possibly arrive at some definite decision, and the
deputation withdrew to untie itself.
Nervous sufferers will tiud it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
19 of this issue.
A Victorian town is in the throes of an
amusing scandal. A while back several of the
leading local lights subscribed towards a locket-
and-chain presentation for the local mounted
policeman, who was about to be transferred to an
even calmer “beat.” When the constable’s bit of
jewellery came to be carefully weighed and con-
sidered, the subscribers, as a body, were dissatisfied
with their purchase, and several excited meetings
took place. The town couldn’t see sufficient
locket and chain for its money, whilst the parties
who laid out the cash refused to submit a state-
ment of accounts. Result, summonses against the
collectors and the jeweller who supplied the goods
for conspiring to defraud the beneticiare of “ £5,
or more.” For the defence it is said that a con-
siderable amount of subscription money was
“blewed” in the process of canvassing hotel-
keepers, and a receipt for a larger sum than the
jewellery cost was given in order to provide funds
for the expense account. Moral—never make
presentations to policemen.
In the window of a religious bookseller in
Pitt-street, Sydney, is a map of Australia show-
ing in blood-red the various 0. of E. missions to
the heathen. There are red marks in various parts
of Queensland and N.S. Wales, but Woolloomoo-
100 and Wexford-street, Sydney, are unmarked ;
likewise Durand’s-alley and the Rocks.
“ Charlie ” Murray, who accompanied
Moran, M.P., tp Perth as delegate of the Yilgarn
(White Feather) cementers and was so successful,
is an old Victorian who, when a compositor in the
Gov. Printing-office, took a prominent' part in
opposing the unjust computing system then in
vogue.

“Is your mother at home ?” inquired the old
parson, as he took his long-accustomed seat in his
best parishioner’s drawing-room.
“ Oh, no, she went to Queensland, last week, to
stay with Effie—l thought you knew.” The old
man leant forward : he was very deaf.
A discreet lady-caller here interjected a remark
about the weather, but it was no use, for he
answered, beamingly—
‘‘Dear Effie—well, well, I am glad—boy or
girl ?”
The discreet lady glanced in despair at her com-
panions. The gentleman visitor gazed fixedly
into space—the young daughter of the house
seemed flurried.
“ Well, really—you see, we don’t quite—l mean
it’s neither.”
“Eh ?”
“ Neither !”
“Neither? Really? You don’t mean—well,
that’s very ex ”
There was a violent ring at the door-bell, and
the girl fled : a moment later she returned.
“It’s BO'IH!” she said, holding out the tele-
grain.
RAGGLES.
Lady clerks now in the Bank of England.
The Maoriland old - age pension scheme
turns up again in a new shape. A New York
paper calmly announces that,(iovernmentis “con-
sidering a scheme for poisoning all residents after
they have attained their seventieth year.”
Apparently it sees nothing remarkable about such
an idea, and regards it as just the sort of thing
that might be reasonably expected in Maoriland.
Any horrible thing may happen quite naturally,
provided it happens a long way off, wherefore
many Australians still believe in the S.M.
Herald’s great fiction of a year or two back
about the execution of 130 priests, in one gory
batch, in the city of Mexico.
An American has lately been travelling
Victoria giving the following diplomas, which
undertakers are exhibiting in their windows :—•
The Australian School of Embalming.—This is to
certify that Mr. Blank, of , has attended a course
of instruction and practical demonstration, and has
qualified himself for embalming and preserving the dead.
In witness whereof this certificate is issued,
Melb., Dec., ’94. Lecturer and Demonstrator.
The chief practical benefit sought is that the
decomposition of Victorian corpses may be
delayed pending the arrival of friends for the
funeral; or during transit by rail. Wanted next
some ’cute Yank to arrest the decomposition of
certain Melbourne financial live bodies.

Conneetieut, U.S., has got a patent hang-
ing machine which strings up the subject auto-
matically when he steps on the trap; but local
parsons object to the labor-saving appliance, be-
cause it flies in the face of Providence, inasmuch
as it “ compels the criminal to be guilty of his own
death.” Society compels scores of innocent people
to be guilty of their own death weekly, and yet
the parsons are with society all the time. If the
Connecticut criminal didn’t walk on to the patent
trap,, he would probably be prodded on, and so is
no more guilty of his own death than the Aus-
tralian crank who walks calmly up to the gallows
instead of lying down and compelling officialdom
to drag him up by the heels. Straight-out legal
murder secures the blessing of the “cludgy,” but
a suggestion of suicide gives them moral horrors.
The man who takes the Scriptures literally ceases
to be a reasoning creature. The Connecticut auto-
matic-hanging system simply means that the
hangee is “ told to resign.”


N.S.W. Fresh Food and Ice
Company, Limited .
Purity is the watchword of the N.S.W. Fresh
Food and Ice Company. Its life is a system :
steam its spirit; 35 engines for ever driving ! night
and day the whirr and roar of the big machinery!
It has the largest refrigerating plant in Austral-
asia, and while its outer buildings consume in the
blaze of the summer’s noon, the roofs and walls of
its inner chambers are heaped with tons of ice and
snpw. High and low, through handsome engine-
rohm and spacious food-store, through cosy office
and ice-bound vault, shine the brilliant eyes of the
electric-light.
The late T. S. Mort founded the Company in
’•6l, investing in it, altogether, <£250,000. But not
until ’74 was the Company
(formally incorporated under
'its present title. Two sons
•of the founder—Mr, Laid-
Hey Mort and Mr. William
Mort—are among the present
directors*
The front b u ildings at
head-quarters face Harbour-
street, and the ice-chambers,
store-rooms, work-shops, en-
gine-rooms, stables, yards,
and the rest stretch back
over Several acres to the
wharves and railway-station
of Darling Harbour. The
land, in fact, was long ago
reclaimed from the waters
of the sea. Captain Broom-
field, one of the directors,
who came to Sydney in ’42,
remembers how the salt
water flowed right over
where the buildings are now,
•and even up to Harbour-
street itself.
In the matter of railway
c image and water carriage,
bills Company is singularly
I Ijst. Tire line runs straight
ii t) the yard, and the trucks
with their loads of milk, or
b jef, or mutton, wheel to
the very doorways. This,
says Captain Broomfield,
means a saving per 1 annum
of many thousands of
pounds.
At the wharves the yards
of the ships’ masts almost
touch the_roof of one build-
i"g, so that > oft cold and hungry days the
sailor; climbing to the mast - head can look
down into the most tempting of bakeries
and get eye- surfeiting visions of substantial
pies and seductive pasiry; or, when the sum-
mers sun is splitting his head, he can watch
the frosted turncocks and 16ng to lay his brow
against thbm and to chew icy items. Refrigera-
tion is not always refrigeration; it is the heaven
of the ultra-convivialist.
» * »
Among the customers of the N.S.W. .Railway
Dept., the Company ranks as one of the largest.
It is, therefore,: no surprise thslt thefseeretmy, Mr.
Fotheringham, speaks with glowing appreciation
of Mr. Eddy. • “ Prior to his management, our
trucks Whuld, be tied in a knot at Redfern ; our
troubles with the railways were frequent and dis
heartening. But late years have brought about
happy changes, and, when Mr. Eddy accepted a
further seven years of office, we promptly con-
him. He acknowledged with cordial
recognition. But I expect he’ll understand that
we congratulated ourselves the most.”
Every day trains from Ri verstone rush in with
tons upon tons of mutton and beef ; while from
the pleasant district of Illawarra, and the fresh
country of Picton and Camden, come thousands of
gallons of milk and cream. The milk-trucks, with
the exception of wheels ami springs, were built by
the company’s workmen in the yard ; they are
fitted with Westinghouse brakes, to run on
passenger trains; the company owns them and
keeps them in repair. Their construction ensures
perfect .ventilation, for in the milk business this is
a most arbitrary provision ; the empty milk-cans
are always returned with their lids off. Each
truck is divided into a top and bottom compart-
ment, and thus carries two tiers of milk-cans.
After delivering its load of milk, a truck is
cleansed throughout with water and steam.
The meat trucks are not sectioned; otherwise
they are built like the milk trucks. Being exclu-
sively devoted to the company’s business, they
are kept scrupulously clean, but they belong to
their builders, the Government Railways Depart-
ment. So do the ice waggons—massive air-tight
packing oases, holding inside their 4-inch thick
walls six solid frozen tons. Over all the sun-
scorched lines of the N.S. W. railways go the ice-
waggons with their cooling loads.
The milk and butter expert supervises hf»
department with vigilant circumspection. He and
his efficient staff of inspectors selected the farms
on which the Company relies for its milk supply,
and at frequent, though wisely irregular, intervals
they thoroughly examine farm and cattle and
farmers. These healthy farms are situated in the
Picton, Camden and Illawarra districts ; granted
good seasons to each district, they produce about
the same quality milk.
Healthy farms, healthy cows, healthy farmers
should, in laymen’s logic, ensure healthy milk;
but the milk expert is on the alert for natural
deteriorating influences, which are both subtle and
unexpected. “ Weeds grow apace.” On the best
of farms they' will spring up in the night, and the
best of cows, eating them, will give milk unfit for
food. But Nature has a worse freak, for the
changed herbage that a cow gets by being turned
from one good paddock into another will some-
times generate in her stomach a gas which, taint-
ing the milk, brings the expert’s condemnation
upon it. The watchfulness of the inexorable
expert does not cease with the conviction that
milk is free from deleterious elements; he requires
it, on being tested, to reach the Company’s, not
the Govt., standard.
Here is the contrast
Solids Fats
English or N.S.W. Government
standard 875 3'oo
N.S.W. Fresh Food and Ice
Company’s standard ... 9'50 360
to 4 - 00
The expert objects to the 88| per cent, of water in
the Govt, standard ; a praiseworthy objection !
The art of eliminating chance is exemplified in
the stringent conditions imposed by the Company
A BLOCK OF ICE.
Mr. PATESON,
Manager.
Mr. FREW,
Chief Engineer,
Capt. BROOMFIELD,
One Director,
upon its farmers. It accepts only such milk as
satisfies the expert’s test; it pays only for milk
so accepted ; it can reject milk without incurring
obligation with the farmer. Further wise arrange-
ments, systematically speaking, complete this
elaborate and perfect supervision.
Of course, such scientific care brings a splendid
reward ; for, though milk affections are numerous
and insidious; though Government inspectors
have, over and over again, investigated all the
workings and methods of the business, and, with-
out hint or wink, suddenly analysed the milk at
farm, railway-station, head-quarters, and cart
delivery ; though never a week passes in Sydney
without some dairyman being fined at the Police
Court for selling adulterated, impure, or inferior
milk, the N.S.W. Fresh Food and Ice Company,
in all its long and useful history, has never once
appeared as a delinquent.
A SECTION OF A MEAT-CHILLING ROOM.
Ou the farms, the milk is first reduced in tem-
perature by the “Lawrence Cooler” to 50° in
winter and to 65° in summer. Then it is run into
the transit cans, and conveyed to the local railway
stations, where the Company’s employes receive
it, place it in the trucks, and guard it upon the
train. Arriving in Sydney, it first undergoes the
severe testing process, and having gained the
expert’s approval, it is poured into the great tanks
of the cooling-chamber, and, in one hour, brought
down to 32°, the freezing point of water, 4° more
than the freezing point of milk. It is then ready
for delivery.
To pass from the rush and traffic of an Aus-
tralian city and the dusty furnace of an Australian
summer into the chamber where the milk is chil-
ling, soothes the senses and charms the soul. The
pure rich milk is within a few inches of the tops of
the great tanks, and fills the chamber with its
delicious sweetness. The refreshing cold gives
sun-scorched, jaded Nature fresh lease of hope and
life; above, below, on every side the air is freezing
and the electric-light is flashing over snow-covered
ceilings and pipes and pillars of whitened cold.
At the chamber’s end, where the ice and snow are
never disturbed, they have formed for themselves
a fantastic grotto, glistening with pendents and
delicate wreaths.
The cooling-tanks are of steel, tin-plated ; the
“agitators ” in them keep the cream equally dis-
tributed ; after each lot of milk has been cooled
and run off, the tanks are cleansed with water and
steam. (9team alone would harden the milk left
about them).
The milk for the service of the ocean-liners and
other steamers, is frozen solid in 3gal. tins. It is
first subjected to a “sterilising process,” which is
nothing more or less than scalding, and prevents
the milk, when being frozen into solid blocks,
from separating into two elements—one the water,
the other the fats and solids.
The milk for suburban morning delivery is
carried from head-quarters to the suburban depots
by waggons which leave each morning at 3 o’clock;
while for the city delivery over 60 milk-carts are
constantly employed. These milk-carts, whose
clean appearance inside and out tells the tale of two
daily washings with water and steam and thorough
ventilation afterwards, have been built upon the
premises. They take two-thirds of their milk
round in the early morning, and the remaining
third in the afternoon. Should a cart bring back
any of its milk, that milk is never sent out again.
In everything connected with the carts, their
horses, and drivers—details so minute as to be too
tedious in narration —there is seen the same
unceasing system, the perfection and precision of
one section of a great elaborate machine. No
wonder that the Company’s milk reputation
spreads far and wide ! No wonder that it sends
milk daily a whole summer through from Sydney
to Bourke 1 No wonder its milk is consumed on
the sumptuous tables of palatial steamers in every
ocean and sea of the globe !
# • •
The butter-making department is ruled over
by the expert with all the rigor and discretion of
his practical wisdom. The cream comes from the
Company’s own creameries; the expert tests it for
purity and quality ; he pays the farmer for it on
its butter value.
The cream is never touched by even a finger’s
tip; it is churned by “ Evenden’s lightning
churn,” and the latest butter-making machines
complete the process. Weights and scales are
dispensed with ; a machine cuts the butter in-
tended for N.S.W. use into pounds and half-
pounds, and stamps upon it the Golden Star and
the Company’s name. This butter is wrapped in
the “ Golden Star ” paper—a vegetable parch-
ment, tasteless, odorless and grease-proof—and
placed in a chilling chamber where the temperature
is 38°. Tons are made daily, and daily a consign-
ment is sent to Wollongong.
The butter for export is packed by the solid
half-hundredweight in square boxes lined with
the vegetable parchment, and is frozen hard as a
stone. These boxes, which are received 50,000 at
a time, are made of New Zealand white pine, and
the wood, having been seasoned, kiln-dried and
machine-dressed, gives off neither taste nor odour,
and is not susceptible to mildew.
The Company’s butter-milk and “ skim-milk ”
are given to many charities.
* # •
The company’s beef, mutton, and pork undergo
the crucial examination of the meat expert; one
diseased or faulty carcase would not only ruin a
whole frozen flock, but would put an ugly blotch
upon the company’s unblemished reputation.
The dressed sheep, on arriving by train, are
passed, 24 at a time, suspended on bars, straight
from the meat-truck to the ingenious weighing-
machine ; they average from 50 to 551 b. each;
now and then one will weigh 1001 b. They are
never handled by the employes; after being
weighed they travel suspended to overhead wheels
right into the chilling-room, where the freezing
process begins. The temperature of this room
when empty is 25°; and, as dressed sheep average
70°, and as 1500 are put in during the short space
of 3a hours, the room’s temperature rises to 45°.
By the end of 24 hours, however, the room is
down to 29°, and the sheep to 33°. The latter
temperature is taken by running a knife into the
thickest part of the leg, and inserting in the orifice
a special form of thermometer. The knife comes
out of a frozen sheep quite dry. At 33° of tem-
perature the carcases are transferred to the
freezing rooms, where they gradually freeze down
to 15°. Then they go into the store-room, where
the biting air registers s°, and where they are
literally buried in a frozen vault. Here they are
bagged and stacked in the same fashion as in the
refrigerating chambers of the ocean liners, and
they are so hard that a knife point will not pene-
trate them. Although sheep can be frozen in 12
hours, the slow and thorough process adopted
takes from two to three days.
Beef to be frozen is cut into quarters, but the
trade in beef is nothing like so brisk as that in
mutton, and while the frozen sheep are numbered
in tens and tens of thousands, the quarters of beef
can be reckoned by the hundreds. Hence one
cold-chamber suffices for all the needs of beef-
freezing, which, with an ordinary quarter, takes a
week.
5° Fahrenheit is an ominous number, but it must
be felt to be realised. Notwithstanding the in-
tensity of the cold, the Company’s Australian-born
workmen stand it remarkably well. Like the other
workmen, they wear chaff-baps around their boots
to protect their feet; and, so fortified, they stick to
their work as Laplanders might, and stack frozen
sheep and blocks of ice for hours and hours a day,
year in and year out.
• * #
The horses which draw the milk-carts and
waggons so briskly are broken by employes. A
horse which is broken in the country needs extra
breaking in town to get used to trains, so the Com-
pany buys the green colt, puts him into double
harness and introduces him at once to the Sydney
street railways. All the harness that the horses
wear is of the Company’s make. A walk round
the yards shows the well-groomed horses in their
long line of stables, the splendid white buggy
horse which is driven by the manager, Mr. Pate-
son, the blacksmith’s shop, the hay store, the
granaries, the harness room, the cart factory, and
the harness-making establishment. In another
part are piles of new-washed cans with their lids
off for ventilation ; and rows of clean carts ready
to go out for the afternoon delivery.
• # «
The bakery supplies the Company’s restaurants
at 92 King-street and 23 Royal Arcade. There
the honest toiler may regale himself with sausages,
bread and tea for 6d., while admiring the hand-
some furniture and decorations, which, in the
King-street shop, cost .£6OOO.
The Bakery has ovens of the most approved
type, each lift, by 13ft., and lighted with elec-
tricity in mica-faced lamps. Science, always
turned to practical account, has produced inven-
tions for shelling almonds, cleaning raisins, &c.
All the pastry is machine-made.
Thus human hands do not taint the goods—not
even the dexterous ones of the chief cook, who,
at the time of visit, was delicately ornamenting
the icing of a wedding-cake. Said he, by way of
explanation, “ No one who buys our wedding-
cake or other edibles ever gets divorced ! Do you
know why? Well, after a man and his wife are
made one, if they use our goods their food digests,
and each feels self is that one; but if they eat
things that won’t digest, one or other discovers
that they are 2, and fancies that he or she is that
2. What trouble comes ! and how simple is the
whole secret!”
This cook should sit at the right hand of Divorce
Justice Windeyer; for his side-light thrown upon
divorce, revealing it in all the frightful horror of a
phase of indigestion, is worthy of scientific inves-
tigation, A Judge or a crocodile might shed
tears over it, but the tears would be tears of joy.
The Company’s fish and game shop, in King-
street, is one of the finest shops in the whole
street, but scarcely one person in a thousand who
sees it is aware that the shop-window, with its
unique display, is really a cold chamber, consum-
ing per day hundreds of pounds of ice.
* * #
New South Wales is dotted with the Company’s
agents ; they are in every suburb of Sydney, and
in every country town; and the Company has
often been asked to open business in Melbourne
and Adelaide. It monopolises the .English,
American, and Maoriland fresh fish and game
trade; the Orient steamship Ophir is specially
fitted for its service; so are the Union Company’s
steamers running to and from Maoriland ports,
while the Arawa brings the Company American
fish from ’Frisco. It supplies nearly all the poul-
try, fish, milk, butter, and ice used by the steam-
ship companies. It freezes for anyone, and intends
to revolutionise the ice-trade. A strange pecu-
liarity of Sydney and N.S.W. people is their
inability to realise the value of ice as a preserver
of fish, meat, milk, and butter.
The Company goes strongly along the high road
of success; between the sth Jan. and sth Feb. it
sent 78,000 frozen sheep to London, whence the
manager, Mr. Pateson, has just returned after an
eight months’ business trip. It is continually
adding to its buildings; a butter store-room with
a capacity of 100 tons has just been completed,
and a new milk-refrigerating plant of 8 tanks, to
carry 22,000 gallons, has been in work but a day
or two.
Many visitors inspect the works, but consider-
ing how interesting inspection proves, how the
Company grants every facility on mere application
at the office, and how Sydney’s population is fast
approaching the half-million, the many visitors
are comparatively few.
• # •
The engines are all new ; the oldest claims but
nine years’ service. The effect at night in the
best ice-engine room is almost dazzling when the
electric-light plays upon the three splendid engines
with their handsome finish and sparkling polish.
Two of them bear the stamp of De La Vergne,
and each will make daily 40 tons of ice or freeze
2000 sheep. The third is the latest addition ; a
beautiful 30-ton model designed by Mr. Auldjo,
and built at the works of the Mort’s Dock
Engineering Company. The ice-engines are five
altogether; they can make 70,000 tons of ice a
year.
The Company has been sending hares and
rabbits for the past three seasons to London, and
at the time of visit these could be seen in one cold
chamber by frozen tens of thousands. It is on
the premises here that the famed Maoriland blue
cod is smoked—with sawdust only.
Local fish, game and poultry, meant for speedy
consumption in the home market, are not placed
in a very cold chamber, but it is cold enough for a
Polar bear in the chamber where the stiff Van-
couver salmon is set off with white, and where the
boxes of Maoriland white bait are encrusted with
snow. Here, side by side, are seen English hali-
but, turbot, kippers, herrings and bloaters;
Maoriland flounders, soles and blue cod ; Ameri-
can salmon from Vancouver and Sacramento.
In another cold chamber are Maoriland venison,
pheasants and quail, while Australian black-duck,
teal and pigeon fill the poor human soul with in-
finite longing and overwhelm the temperance of
the ordinary mind with the wild mad joy of the
epicure.
# # *
The ice store-room contains some hundreds of
tons of ice—just a goodly slab of the North Pole
lit up by the electric-light, instead of by a six
months’ sun or the Aurora Borealis. An old man,
thin and dried (for he has worked here eight hours
a day for 20 years, and refrigeration is to some
extent dessication), is stacking ice with bare hands.
His boots are thickly wrapped around with chaff-
bags to protect his feet from the intense cold;
close beside him peeps the handle of a shovel from
its bed of snow.
He is well and vigorous and cheery; he has only
been ill once in the 20 years—that was when he
worked in the sun one day and caught the very
devil of a strange illness. He loves the ice-room,
he says ; and while he is speaking the visitor bolts
suddenly for the door. Those who wish to know
more may go and find out, go and yarn with
the lively Esquimaux preserved in his ice-cave, go
and yarn and stack ice, and get fingers and nose
and feet frost-bitten, as one foolhardy investi-
gator did heretofore.

Melbourne detectives say that any number of:

ismellbourne employers refuse to prosecute their cronk
Clerks, °r travel ers, for two reasons. Firstly, they object-

„ A Mullewa (Westralia) hotel advertises that

* * * *
Ross (M.L.) has a veteran prospector—a Portu-
guese named Zala—who owns a mine on one of the
highest p -aks of the Alps, where hardly any other man
could get a footing. He is the whole staff of the mine
and has run the entire show single-handed from the
beginning. During seven years he has driven 1200 ft. of
hard rock tunnel, and has opened up a reef yielding an
average of about 2oz. When he gets an air-shaft put
down, m another year or two, the old man expects to
start crushing and make a big fortune, if he doesn’t fall
off the roof of his property In the Interval and roll right
into the town of Boss, four miles away. Then, when he
has made his pile he will probably buy n mountain of his
own and settle down to enjoy nn honored old age all
alone. A man who has led a solitary life on top of an
Alp, for ten years or so, doesn’t care for society.
* * m *
A clerk, in a boom builder’s office, when prices
were rising all round, used, when a buyer made default,
get him to make a transfer to the clerk, pay up the
balance, and sell. As the builder’s terms were always 12J
per cent, deposit, balance monthly instalments, the clerk
did well; but, when his method was discovered, the
employer protested with his boot.

A Sydney merchant, some years ago, bought
a property in George-street, Sydney. A couple of days
afterwards he srld it at cent, per cent, profit against the
advice of his friends. Two days later he found that his
purchaser had sold it to the N.S.W. Govt, as a site for a
public building, and cleared £4OOO out of the deal, and
that merchant w'ent out and kicked himsejf badly.
» • • •
A storekeeper bad customers who would give
their orders, call on their return journey, take the goods,
and pay cash. After a year he sold out, and a demand
for payment was received by the cash customers for the
year’s supply. As they had no receipts to show against
his hooks, they had again to pay. It was worked cun-
ningly, as only one was tackled at a time. In that town-
ship, now, when a man gets his hair cut he takes a receipt
for payment from the barber.

The books of Coburg (Vic.) Shire Council have
disappeared, and now the councillors are trying hard to
remember the names of the people who owe £3OOO in
rates, and are succeeding very badly. Every man in the
-shire says that he called and paid up shortly before the
■disappearance, and for the first time on record there
doesn’t seem to be one penny of arrears in the district.

A Talk with an Optician.
An optician who has fitted spectacles upon vice-
regal noses; whose lenses give normal sight to the
60 year-old eyes of Gov. Norman; who has
ensured accurate vision to the weather-eye of
prophet Wragge; who has rectified the unusual
combination of ■ presbyopia; astigmatism, and
myopia that troubles Chief Justice Samuel
Griffith, ought to be a scientist in his profession.
And the dignitaries quoted regard Mr. Greenfield
as an unmixed blessing : they send their friends to
him and give him glowing testimonials to be used
at his discretion. There is scarcely a prominent
public man in Queensland —from, the Governor,
Griffith, M‘llwraith, Lilley, andi Ministers of
I'm Crown to justices, doctors, I lawyers, and
M.’sP.—who does not wear a ; pair of this
optician’s glasses. So, while his partner, Mr.
Barraclougb, manages the Brisbane branch of the
business, Mr, Greenfield ought to reap success at
the establishment he has started in Sj r dney.
The glasses which Chief Justice Griffith wears
when’on the Bench have probably no fellow of
Australian make; they do the work of three
different pairs of glasses. The upper part of each
lens corrects the astigmatism and myopia of
vision, and enables the Chief Justice to survey the
whole court; the lower portion rectifies the pres-
byopia and astigmatism, rendering the writing
and reading of notes a normally easy task. The
Chief Justice’s fine testimonial certifies that Mr.
Greenfield is a competent optician, in whom the
public may place confidence, and no decision ever
given by a Chief Justice could have graver portent
than this has for one special section of suffering
humanity.
The eye, whose miraculous construction and
beauty of adjustment fills the inquiring mind with
fear and wonder, is treated by public ignorance
and unprincipled speculation with a clumsy
callousness nothing short of criminal. The man
who in the mad rage of personal combat strikes
his opponent blind, is summarily dealt with by
law. But the cold-blooded pretender, who calls
himself an optician, may prescribe spectacles and
glasses to a helpless community, may pocket his
fees, and laugh with impunity at the misery he
causes. f
The offences of such unqualified practitioners
have become so loud a scandal in England that
the profession’sheading representatives are form-
ing themselves into a society to fix a standard of
examination, which shall "be submitted for the
approval of Parliament, with the intention of
getting a bill passed for exterminating the charla-
tan from the opticians’ ranks.
Mr. Greenfield has this movement most dearly
at heart, and keeping himself in touch with the
leading Englishmen of his profession, he intends,
tlie moment that the needed law is enacted in
England, to use it as a precedent and try for a
similar enactment in Australia.
Some of the saddest phases of human ignorance
and folly are brought under the notice of the pro-
fessional optician. Mr. Greenfieild has chanced
upon 13 or 14 men and women, who, staring with
the naked eye at the sun during the last transit of
Venus, produced atrophy of the optic nerve, end-
ing in total blindness. He could: show thejintem-
perate lover of drink and tobacco more than one
blind beggar whose dismal plight is directly due
to atrophy of the optic nerve, primarily induced
by excessive drinking and smoking.
He grinds all his lenses, manufactures all his
spectacles, fitting them to individual sight and
form of face, thus securing all possible comfort for
the wearer. He has spent nearly 20 years in
sight-testing, and he was the first to introduce
his scientific method to Australia. His system is
so complete that, granted the eyes are free from
disease, a person’s exact age can be ascertained
by it.
Mr. Greenfield holds forth very warmly upon
‘‘ rule of thumb ” tests and the farcical examina-
tion conducted by pretended experts, and he de-
nounces the nonsensical toy employed by some
operators to delude the public—“ petty tricks and
artifices which would be ridiculous but for the
seriousness of the evils that they cause.”
“ You can understand how much simplicity and
trust are at the mercy of the charlatan when I tell
you that scores and scores of people come to me
convinced that their eyes are diseased or defective,
and who, after due inspection without charge, go
away rejoiced to know that their eyes are normal,
and that their only want is a short holiday in the
country, or at sea, to rejuvenate a jaded system.
Charlatans’ victims wearing concave lenses realised
with a start the danger they were running when I
changed these lenses for convex ones.
“There are people whose sight will be perfect for
another 20 years, coming to be examined every six
months. One man with remarkably good sight
was under the delusion he was going blind.
People working in a bad light get eye affections
which disappear when proper lighting is allowed
them.
“More men than women wear spectacles in Aus-
tralia, but, of the two, women are perhaps the
more credulous. Nothing - could exceed the inno-
cence of a young lady I attended the other day ;
she attributed her myopia (short-sight) to an in-
growing toe-nail 1 Not a few simple loving
mothers associate their child’s squint with asuper-
stition, or think it is wholly the effect of measles
or whooping-cough, or of looking at a bow upon a
bonnet; and they are very grateful if I explain the
common cause of squint, which is hypermetropia,
or over-sight.
“ Some folks, who would look upon a person wear-
ing a tight boot as a great fool, put colored glasses
upon their own noses and permanently injure their
eye-sight. They have a craze for self-experiment
upon the most delicate and precious of the organs;
they use deep-black shades, when they need only
the faintest tinge of color, and even that only
under irritating conditions such as the midday
glare and dust. With proper colored glasses the
eyes, when possible, grow stronger until they are
restored to their normal power, and then the
glasses are discarded altogether.
“Human eyes have strange freaks. One Sydney
clergyman has odd eyes ; he looks at the con-
gregation with his right eye, while he reads the
Bible or prayer-book with the left. Sir Arthur
Palmer, of Brisbane, has eyes similar to these.
One whimsical optical defect is double vision—
unfortunately mated in the lay mind with splen-
did conviviality. But it is a sad thing when the
soberest of men always sees two lamp-posts in-
stead of one, or mistakes one sovereign for two
pounds sterling. I have overcome the embarras-
sing failing in four people during the last few
weeks.”
Mr. Greenfield speaks with deep commiseration
of the North-West Queenslanders, about Blackall
and Aramac, where the sand-fly pest rages summer
after summer, and sandy-blight brings wretched-
ness upon man and beast. Another Queensland
feature that struck him was the number of men
whose eyes had been injured in blasting accidents.
He made a pair of spectacles for one lucky unfor-
tunate, whose vision escaped, while the whites of
his eyes were blackened with burnt - powder
grains. “ I never saw or heard of such a case.
How his sight was preserved can only be explained
as a miracle. ”
. “The obviously natural cause of defective vision
is the stealing on of age. People who follow
country avocations, and those engaged in manual
labor, develope premature defects of vision only
as rare exceptions to a rule. Sailors and farmers
have exceptionally good long-range vision.
“Some people tinker with their eyes as if they
had two or three dozen pairs to fall back upon
should they destroy the ones in use. We never
charge for testing a person’s sight, yet youths of
23 or 24 will go and select so-called pebble spec-
tacles from amongst a job stock, wear them, and
cripple their power of vision for ever. One 23-
year-old youth I found wearing spectacles which
would suit his eyes when he reaches the age of
eighty. The common, so-called pebble spectacle
is wicked trash that injures the eye more than
ordinary ‘ window-pane ’ glasses do.”
Myopia, or short-sight, is a defect whose symp-
toms are conveyed in the popular name. Hyper-
metropia, or oversight, may escape its victim’s
consciousness for a considerable time; then the
great strain of the eye-muscles brings on headache,
eye-ache, inflammation of the eyes, trouble when
reading, double vision, and .other distresses.
Astigmatism is chat odd affliction (which makes
the upright line of a black cross look blacker than
the horizontal line, or vice versa. It will cause a
picture of uniform brightness to appear brighter
in places than in others.
Asthenopia, weakness of the converging mus-
cles, or muscles which balance the eye, may allow
vision to seem perfect in a room or in the open air,
but reading continuously for five minutes or more
causes dimness of sight, the running and blurring
of words together, attended with irritation or pain.
Photophobia is a weakness of the retina, which
betrays itself in fear of bright light, and the mani-
festation of a desire to shield the eye from the
slightest glare.
Such are a few of the chief defects, as briefly
outlined by Mr. Greenfield in colloquial terms.
It is in the cities where the students are, where
the clerks and printers work with artificial light,
and where the doctors, barristers and pressmen
often seem to live for mere close application,
that we get the defects known as hypermetropia)
myopia, astigmatism, asthenopia, photophobia’
and the 52 diseases of the eve.
“‘Malformation can be easily induced in the eyes
of children of tender age, by keeping them poring
over books and lessons when they should be out
in the open, giving their eyes the healthy stimulus
of exercise at different foci. I have made glasses
for many such little sufferers.
“ Cle.nent myopia is only slight, but I
saw an extreme case the other day in a lady who
could not tell her daughter by sight 18in. away.
I prescribed her a pair of spectacles, and she now
recognises people quite clearly upon the other side
of a wide street.'
The manufacture and adjustment of artificial
eyes forms a big feature of Mr. Greenfield’s prac-
tice, and, granted a socket in good order, he says
he can put in an artificial eye with such natural
effect that it will defy the discrimination of a
quick detective.
Mr. Greenfield intends very soon to go on a
part-professional tour through the
.. - \
principal towns along the Southern line of the
N.S.W. railways.
lie keeps a register of all measurements taken,
so that any patron breaking or losing spectacles
prescribed by him has only to notify Mr. Green-
field, in order to get a similar pair. „ |

The Lisniore (N.S.W,) Police Magistrate
refuses to allow a verdict for payment of drinks
supplied to their employes by hotelkeepers. The
back blocks are studded with pubs., all the carpen-

received a coin for their labor, and were generally
shown to be a few pounds in arrears at the end. of

IMPRISONED ON A SHIP.
The steamship Normannia, from Hamburg,
arrived in the port of New York orlSaturday,
Sept. 3rd, 1892, with eases of cholera on board.
Many of the ship’s company.bad died; on ..the. pas-
sage. At Hamburg and elsewhere in Europe the
disease was raging. The authorities in America
were alarmed lest the scourge should he introduced (
into that country, Hence they quarantined the,
Normannia, with every soul of her passengers and i
crew. The writer was a passenger, nlt was an,
awful time. Death was among us and on aUi
sides of us. Nobody knew who next would fall.
We were imprisoned. Liberty never seemed so.
fair, nor so far. We could neither fight nor fly,.
There we were—hundreds of us— perfectly well,
and yet bound together as with chains, that the,
health officer of the port might see whether the,
plague would not yet break out in bur midst..
When at last —after weeks of this—we were set on,
shore, men lifted their hats and reverently said]
“ Thank God! ’’
This was being shut up under conditions Ip.
make it horrible and fearful. Yet, any form of,
incarceration is bad enough. Here is a womans
for example, who says, “ 1 never moved a yard},
jrom my own doorstep for twenty weeks /” I ten ow.im
house was a prison to her. " Who had'sentenced/
her? A judge? No; a power greater and) more
pitiless than any judge.
Her tale runs thus : In April. 1882,'whilst living
at Lasher’s Farm, Old Sampford, Essex, a tin-
broke out, and the family were burned out of
house and home. We have no call to remark on
such a calamity. The very thought of it is fit to
make one shiver with dread. For most of us it is
like the world coming to an end to experience such
a disaster.
Well, what happened after that the lady shall
tell in her own fashion—the best of all fashions,
because it is plain and straight to the point. She
says: “Owing to our bedding being damp from
exposure, I took a bad cold, which brought on
rheumatic fever. For fourteen days I was eon-
fined to my bed, and for twenty weeks I nevet
moved a yard from my own doorstep. Aft. r a
time the fever abated, leaving me weak, languid j
and low. At first I had i sickening taste in the .
mouth, and a poor apt etite. No matter how.-
simple and light the food was, I was afraid tb eat,
for it waasure Jto give.me pain at the chest -andi
sides ; so I often had to loosen my corset and un-
dress myself during the day. I could not bear the.-
weight of my clothing.
“I was constantly spitting up a sour, frothy
fluid, and had a gnawing pain at the pit of the,
stomach—like hunger, and yet different. It waw
with difficulty I voided the kidney secretion, aiuS
my bowels, ankles and legs began to swell. I goit
worse; I was in agony night and day, and eorfd
not put ray foot on the ground, Soon afterwards,
a husky CQugh took me, and my throat filled with
a thick phlegm. I could not sleep, and wag never
easy. Later on, I had often to sit up in bed, for I
felt as if I should choke. i■
“ Year after year L continued t.& suffer in Jthis
way, growing worse and wbrse, utilil I despaired
of ever being well;again. But who can tell when
trouble will come, or when relief? A wonderful
Providence is over aIL . :
“ One day in June, a hook came by post de-
scribing Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup and what
it had done for many poor sufferers. I got a
bottle from Mr. Suckling, medicine dealer, and
after taking it for a short time all pain left me,
and I gradually gained strength. By taking aii
occasional dose I have since kept in good heijtli
and can eat and digest any kind of food. (Signed)
Mrs. Lydia Queen, Moor End, Great Sampford,
via Braintree, Essex, Aug. 24th, 1892.” i 1
Now, in order that Mrs. Green’s clear andl
truthful statement may be of use to others -(as
she desires it to be), We must add a word or two,
The bad cold she caught at the fire no doubt
“brought on” the rheumatic fever (as she •
relates), but there was something back of the
cold, for a cold never causes rheumatism. The ■
rheumatic seeds, or poison, must already lie in ,
the blood ; and that poison is always created by
■pre-existing indigestion and dyspepsia, whether •
the sufferer knows it or not. This is proved by
the fact that Mrs. Green’s chief ailment for ten.
years after the fire was not rheumatism, but
indigestion and dyspepsia and dropsy, which is,
•one of its results and symptoms. When fim-
digestion was finally'righted by the remedy she.
alludes to, all her apparent maladies ceased)
together. Why? Because she had but one, as we.
have said.
Ah, yes. Disease is a stern gaoler. And how
sweet (and cheap) is liberty, obtained by Mother
Saigel’s help.
